1. SUMMARY 
 

During 2011, DNI Metal Inc. ("DNI") released an inferred resource estimate for a 
portion of the Buckton Mineralized Zone ("Buckton inferred maiden resource estimate"), 
which was prepared by APEX Geoscience Ltd. ("APEX") on DNI's poly-metallic black 
shale hosted SBH Property in northeastern Alberta (Dufresne et al., 2011). The Buckton 
inferred maiden resource estimate, which is available at www.sedar.com (October 24, 
2011), calculated the recoverable grades for eight traditional black shale metals/oxides 
including MoO3, Ni, U3O8, V2O5, Zn, Cu, Co and Li2CO3. The estimate reported an 
inferred resource of 226.9 million tonnes (250.1 million short tons) at an average value 
using the recoverable metal grade and 5 year average price for the metals (or oxides) 
for all 8 metals of $USD23.0 per tonne ($US20.9 per short ton). 
 

The polymetallic Mo-Ni-U-V-Zn-Cu-Co-Li Buckton inferred maiden resource of 
Dufresne et al. (2011) remains intact. The intent of this report, however, is to provide a 
supplementary resource estimate that focuses on rare earth elements (REE), yttrium 
(Y), scandium (Sc) and thorium (Th) as co-products to the extraction of traditional 
metals. This supplementary resource estimate is in support of a recent DNI press 
release that discloses positive bioleach extraction results for liberating REE and other 
specialty metals from the Second White Speckled Shale Formation in northeastern 
Alberta (DNI Metals Inc., Press Release, September 8, 2011). No additional drilling to 
the Buckton Mineralized Zone has been completed, but the new metallurgical result 
requires additional assessment of co-products complementary to the original poly-
metallic Buckton inferred maiden resource estimate of Dufresne et al. (2011).  
 

DNI Metal Inc.'s SBH Property is located, approximately 120 kilometers (km) 
north of Fort McMurray, Alberta in the Birch Mountains. The Property consists of 
272,032 hectares (ha) in 36 contiguous Alberta Metallic and Industrial Mineral (MAIM) 
Permits that are held 100% by DNI Metals Inc. (DNI). The MAIM Permits grant DNI the 
exclusive right to explore for metallic and industrial minerals for 14 years subject to 
assessment work performance biannually. Parts of the SBH Property are underlain by 
oil sands at depth.  The rights to coal, oil and gas that might potentially underlie the 
Property are held by other parties under separate permits and licences. DNI's 100% 
interest in the Property is subject only to a traditional royalty retained by the Province of 
Alberta against metal production revenues. There are no other overriding royalties 
encumbering DNI's interest. 
 

The primary exploration target on the Property is poly-metallic metal zones 
hosted in mid-Cretaceous black shale with intercalated bentonite and basal bioclastic 
bone and fish scale bearing sandstone beds. The poly-metallic zones are envisaged to 
extend over vast areas (100's of km

2
), occurring as flat-lying near-surface strata that 

may be amenable to extraction by open pit bulk mining methods where overburden strip 
ratios are sufficiently low to allow economic extraction. Several poly-metallic zones have 
been identified by historic exploration on the DNI Permits, two of which have been 
confirmed by historic drilling. The primary targets are the Buckton mineralized zone and 

http://www.sedar.com/


Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast Alberta Page 2 
 

the Asphalt mineralized zone, both of which are hosted in the Cretaceous Second White 
Speckled Shale Formation (Second White Specks Shale). 
 

Exploration work performed by DNI, and APEX on behalf of DNI, from 2008 to 
2010 consisted of a variety of programs including re-sampling and analysis of historic 
third-party 1997 drill core, field stratigraphic and bone bed sampling, geochemical 
metallurgical testing comprised of sulphuric acid leaching and bioleaching along with a 
subsurface geological and stratigraphic synthesis culminating in subsurface modeling. 
Exploration during 2011, which was completed by personnel from APEX under the 
direct supervision of Mr. Michael Dufresne, P.Geol., A Qualified Person, and one of the 
authors of this Technical Report, consisted of coring a total of eight HQ diameter vertical 
diamond drillholes consisting of three drillholes at the Asphalt Zone and five holes at the 
Buckton Zone. The holes were drilled between January 19

th
 and February 14

th
, 2011 by 

Lone Peak Drilling of Kimberley, British Columbia.  The coring program yielded a total 
648 m in 8 core holes. Seven of the eight drillholes intersected the Second White 
Specks Shale with widths ranging from 13 to 23 m. The eighth drillhole was lost in 
overburden.  All samples were sent to Activation Laboratories Ltd. (Actlabs) of Ancaster, 
Ontario. 
 

From 2009 to 2011, DNI conducted metallurgical test work employing a variety of 
bioleaching and acid leaching tests with the purpose of establishing the leachability of 
the Second White Specks Shale and optimum recovery parameters for a variety of 
metals.  Leaching test work was conducted at the Bureau de Recherches Géologiques 
et Minières (BRGM), France, Alberta Innovates Technology Futures (AITF and formerly 
the Alberta Research Council), Edmonton, Alberta, and Actlabs, Ancaster, Ontario. In 
general, the test work indicates that most of the metals of interest in the Second White 
Specks Shale can be extracted with reasonable recoveries.  
 

In addition to collective recoverability of traditional metals, bioleaching studies 
carried out by AITF confirmed successful extraction of REE including desired elements 
such as Dy, Y, Tb, Eu and Nd, and associated specialty metals Sc and Th. For the most 
part, the test work was conducted on raw surface trench samples and without the 
benefit of optimizing the leaching parameters. It is believed optimizing the leaching 
parameters could improve some of the metal recoveries.  
 

Based upon the results of the 1997 and 2011 diamond drilling programs and the 
Buckton inferred maiden resource estimate (Dufresne et al., 2011), both of which were 
supervised by Mr. Dufresne, DNI retained APEX to prepare a supplementary REE-Y-
Sc-Th resource estimate for a portion of the Buckton Mineralized Zone. The REE-Y-Sc-
Th mineral resource model and estimate was prepared by Mr. Roy Eccles, P.Geol. and 
Mr. Steve Nicholls, MAIG under the direction of Mr. Dufresne. The model was 
generated using the combined drillhole data derived from the 1997 and 2011 drilling 
campaigns. Modeling was constrained to domains interpreted to reflect distinct zones or 
types of REE mineralization. Interpolation characteristics in the resource model were 
defined based on the geology, drillhole spacing and geostatistical analysis of the data 
contained within these domains. Mineral resources were classified in accordance with 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast Alberta Page 3 
 

guidelines established by the Canadian Institute of Mining, Metallurgy and Petroleum 
(CIM) "Estimation of Mineral Resources and Mineral Reserves Best Practice 
Guidelines" dated November 23

rd
, 2003 and CIM "Definition Standards for Mineral 

Resources and Mineral Reserves" dated November 27
th

, 2010.  
 

The Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th mineral resource modeling and estimation was 
carried out using a 3-dimensional block model based on geostatistical applications 
using commercial mine planning software MICROMINE (v12.0.4). A total of 11 diamond 
drillholes were used in the resource estimate.  Spacing between drill holes varies from 
240 m to 2.5 km, with an average distance of about 1 km between drillholes.  There are 
four drill sections that range in spacing from about 670 m to 2 km between sections.  
The sample file comprised 549 samples of variable length from all lithologies but when 
composited in MICROMINE yielded 302 sample composites for the Second White 
Specks Shale that were used for the REE-Y-Sc-Th mineral resource estimate.  Data 
supplied and utilized in MICROMINE included collar easting, northing and elevation 
coordinates, lithology, along with assay data for 17 chemical elements, specifically the 
15 lanthanides (La to Lu) plus scandium and yttrium, as well as bulk density (specific 
gravity) data.  All drillholes were drilled vertically and as such there was no down hole 
survey information collected. 
 

Variography was conducted on the composited drillhole data within the Second 
White Specks Shale domain to produce spherical semi variogram's. Each element was 
modeled individually to determine the continuity and orientation of mineralization.  As a 
result of the wide drillhole spacing's a parent model block size of 250 m x 250 m x 2 m 
was chosen for the Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th block modeling and resource estimate.  Sub-
blocking was conducted down to 25 m x 25 m x 1 m.  The block model was extended far 
enough past the mineralized wireframe to encompass the entire mineralized shale 
domain.   
 

The Buckton Zone REE-Y-Sc-Th mineral resource estimate has been calculated 
utilizing the estimated recoverable grade for each of 17 metals (or oxides thereof):  

• Light rare earth elements (LREE) consisting of La, Ce, Pr and Nd 
• Middle rare earth elements (MREE) consisting of Sm, Eu and Gd 
• Heavy rare earth elements (HREE) consisting of Tb, Dy, Ho, Er, Tm, Yb and Lu 
• Plus Sc and Y, which are considered rare earth elements since they tend to 

occur in the same ore deposits as the lanthanides and exhibit similar 
chemical properties. 

 
This technical report provides the resource for the recoverable REE's. The 

estimated recoverable grade was derived from multiplying the raw estimated grades by 
the estimated recovery percentages based upon metallurgical test work completed to 
date (Table 1.1). The poly-metallic recoverable grades were translated into a $USD 
value for each block and sub-block by multiplying the 1 to 3 year average trailing prices 
for that metal in $USD in order to evaluate the mineral resource estimate (Table 1.1). In 
instances where 3 year average prices were not available (i.e., the HREE), one year 
average prices were adopted.  



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast Alberta Page 4 
 

 
In order to economically evaluate the mineral resource estimate a lower block 

value cut-off of $USD7.5 per tonne was employed for the estimate. This is the same 
cut-off used for the Buckton inferred maiden resource estimate of Dufresne et al. (2011) 
and estimates production for the traditional metals plus REE-Y-Sc-Th as a pregnant 
solution co-product, but it is unclear what additional costs will be incurred to extract and 
refine the REE-Y-Sc-Th metals into a saleable product. Although we provide 
recoverable grades and an estimated value per tonne, there is much less confidence in 
the long term REE pricing, the long term viability of REE supply, what effects new 
production will have on REE pricing, the actual cost of extracting the REE's from a 
pregnant polymetallic solution. 

 
The mineral resource is classified as inferred based upon the CIM Guidelines 

and consists of 226.9 million tonnes (250.1 million short tons) at an average value using 
the recoverable metal grade and one- to three-year average prices for the metal (or 
oxide) for the total rare earth oxides (REO) plus Y2O3 of $USD22.00 per tonne 
($US20.00 per short ton) as displayed in Table 1.1. The total REO,Y2O3 raw grade is 
320 ppm. If Sc2O3 and ThO2 are added, the total 17 element resource becomes 
$USD57.73 per tonne ($US52.37 per short ton). 
 

Recommendations for 2012 exploration include, but are not limited to, three 
fundamental areas for advancement of the SBH Property costing approximately CDN$9 
million. It is important to point out that the mineral resource estimates reported by the 
Buckton inferred Mo-Ni-U-V-Zn-Cu-Co-Li maiden resource estimate and the Buckton 
supplementary REE-Y-Sc-Th mineral resource estimate are for only a portion of the 
Buckton Mineralized Zone. The grade of the Second White Specks seems to increase 
to the untested northern part of the zone and there is every reason to believe that the 
zone of polymetallic-, REE- and specialty metal- enrichment will be extended with 
further drilling to the north and northeast. Subsequently, a 2012 drill program of 40 
drillholes (approximately 5,000 m) is recommended to expand the current inferred 
resource, locate areas of minimal overburden thickness and test whether higher grade 
extensions of the Buckton polymetallic mineralized zone occur to the north. Infill drilling 
should also be used to confirm the continuity of grade and thickness of the Second 
White Specks Shale and bring portions of the resource into a higher category of 
classification (i.e., indicated resource estimate). The expected drilling cost for either 
helicopter supported summer drilling or winter road accessible drilling is an average all-
in cost of $CND1,000/m for a total cost of $CND5.0 million. 
 

In addition, potential analogies between the REE-enriched Second White 
Speckled Shale and REE ion-adsorption clay deposits in southern China are important 
because extraction of REE from ion-adsorption deposits is a relatively straightforward 
process in comparison to other traditional rock-hosted REE deposit sources. Thus, 
continued metallurgical and geological assessment at an estimated total cost of about 
$CND1.5 million is required. Metallurgical testing should include ion-adsorption-type 
REE extraction and bulk sample leaching test work to assess the extractability of the  
 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast Alberta Page 5

2. INTRODUCTION 
 

During 2011, APEX Geoscience Ltd. (APEX) was retained by DNI Metals Inc. 
(DNI) to conduct a core drilling program at its 100% owned SBH Property and construct 
a mineral resource estimate for poly-metallic (Mo, Ni, U, V, Zn, Cu, Co and Li) 
mineralization within a  portion of its Cretaceous Second White Specked Shale-hosted 
Buckton Mineralized Zone in northeastern Alberta. The Buckton inferred maiden 
resource estimate (Dufresne et al., 2011) remains intact and is available at 
www.sedar.com (October 24, 2011).  
 

The key to unlocking the potential of DNI's poly-metallic prospect is to find areas 
with high concentrations of the critical metals including Mo, Ni, U and V. Not to be 
overlooked, however, is the importance of co-products from these very large volume 
black shale deposits. While the Buckton inferred maiden resource estimate included 
Mo, Ni, U, V, Zn, Cu, Co and Li (Dufresne et al., 2011), the intent of this technical report 
is to create a supplementary resource estimate that focuses on the rare earth elements 
(REE) including the 15 lanthanides (La to Lu) plus scandium (Sc) and yttrium (Y).  
 

The industrial application and demand for REE has increased dramatically in the 
last years, Rare earth elements are critical in the production of a variety of high tech and 
green energy technologies. In addition, prices have increased based on concern over 
China's prominent role in the global supply chain for rare earths and the quest for 
alternate sources of supply is becoming more urgent.  
 

DNI's leaching test work over the past two years has confirmed the effective 
recoverability of traditional metals from the Second White Speckled Shale (see 
Dufresne et al., 2011). The test extraction work also reported successful incidental 
recoveries of additional metals of value as potential co-products. These co-products 
consist of rare earth elements (REE) including Dy, Y, Tb, Eu and Nd and specialty 
metals such as Sc and Th. Because any future mine production at DNI's Buckton 
Property should quantify the full resource potential of DNI's Buckton Zone, a 
supplementary mineral resource estimate focusing on REE, Y, Sc and Th is required 
and is the purpose of this report.  
 

The Buckton Zone REE-Y-Sc-Th resource estimate was calculated from drilling 
information and core analysis conducted in 2011 plus resampling and reanalyzing drill 
cores from historical 1997 drilling. Thus, the assay data from both the 2011 and 1997 
drill cores used the identical laboratory methodology and are considered modern and 
robust geochemical datasets. APEX personnel supervised the 1997 and 2011 drilling 
campaigns at the SBH Property. The geological and wire frame modeling was 
completed by Mr. R. Eccles, P.Geol., a Qualified Person with respect to mineral 
resource estimation as defined by National Instrument (NI) 43-101.  The resource 
estimation statistical analysis and block modeling was completed by Mr. S. Nicholls, 
MAIG, a Qualified Person, under the direct supervision of Mr. M. Dufresne, P. Geol. and 
Mr. R. Eccles, P.Geol., who are both Qualified Persons with respect to mineral 
estimation as defined by the Canadian Securities Administration (CSA) NI 43-101. 

http://www.sedar.com/


Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast Alberta Page 8 
 

Mineral resource modeling and estimation was carried out using a 3-dimensional 
block model based on geostatistical applications using the commercial mine planning 
software MICROMINE (v12.0.4). The resource estimate of this intermediate stage 
exploration project is classified as an Inferred Mineral Resource, and was classified in 
accordance with guidelines established by the Canadian Institute of Mining, Metallurgy 
and Petroleum (CIM) "Estimation of Mineral Resources and Mineral Reserves Best 
Practice Guidelines" dated November 23

rd
, 2003 and CIM "Definition Standards for 

Mineral Resources and Mineral Reserves" dated November 27
th

, 2010.  The effective 
date of the mineral resource is December 20, 2011. 
 

Mr. Michael Dufresne, P.Geol., a Qualified Person and the lead author visited the 
SBH Property during field work on June 16

th
, 2010 and during the recent drilling 

campaign on January 23
rd

 and 24
th
, 2011. Mr. Dufresne of APEX has had a long 

involvement with the property and has visited and conducted work on the property on 
numerous occasions including supervising the 1997 historic drilling program at the 
Buckton Mineralized Zone (Sabag, 2010; 2008; 1998) that was conducted by APEX on 
behalf of Tintina Mines Ltd. (Tintina) along with numerous field programs on behalf 
Tintina and the Alberta Geological Survey (AGS) culminating in the publication of AGS 
Special Report 09 "The Geological and Geochemical Setting of the Mid-Cretaceous 
Shaftesbury Formation and Other Colorado Group Sedimentary Units in Northern 
Alberta" (Dufresne et al., 2001).

Alberta Metallic and Industrial Mineral (MAIM) Permits are acquired by application to the 
Minerals Division of Alberta Energy, and the same are granted under the Alberta Mines 
and Minerals Act (Chapter-17), and related Metallic and Industrial Mineral Tenure 
Regulation. Geographic locations of the Permits are defined per the Alberta Township 
System (ATS), a variant of the Dominion Land Survey (DLS), which is based on 
Townships, Ranges, Sections and subdivisions thereof. 
 

A number of the original MAIM Permits were originally acquired in 2008 by Mr. 
Sabag, President and CEO of DNI (formerly Dumont Nickel Inc.; Dumont Nickel Inc. 
News Release, April 16, 2008). The Permits were subsequently transferred at no cost to 
DNI in August of that year once land assembly was completed and DNI had secured the 
necessary corporate registrations in the Province of Alberta. Alberta MAIM Permits can 
be held by an individual person, or by any organized or corporate entity, which is duly 
registered to do business in the province Alberta. Subsequently in May of 2010, Dumont 
Nickel Inc. consolidated its shares and changed its name to DNI Metals Inc. (DNI Metals 
Inc. Press Release dated May 10, 2010).  A search of the all 36 MAIM Permits at 
Alberta Energy indicates that all the permits are active, are in good standing and are 
unencumbered. 
 

The Buckton Mineralized Zone is contained within Permits 9308060410 and 
9308060412 (Table 4.1 and Figure 4.1).  The Buckton South Mineralized Zone is 
contained within Permit 9308060408 and the Asphalt Mineralized Zone is contained 
within Permits 9308060407 and 9309010692 (Table 4.1 and Figure 4.1). 
 
4.2 - PROPERTY RIGHTS AND MAINTENANCE  
 

The MAIM Permits grant DNI the exclusive right to explore for metallic and 
industrial minerals for seven consecutive two-year terms (total of fourteen years), 
subject to traditional assessment work performance biannually. Work requirements for 
maintenance of the permits in good standing are $5/ ha for the first term, $10/ha for 
each of the second and third terms, and $15/ha for each the fourth, fifth, sixth and 
seventh terms. 
 

The Alberta Mines and Minerals Act and the Alberta Metallic and Industrial 
Mineral Tenure Regulations provide for the accumulation of excess work in any term for 
filing toward subsequent terms, and also enable the reduction of permit areas during 
their currency. There are no statutory provisions for the renewal of permits beyond their 
14 year term. The statutes also provide for conversion of Permits to Metallic Minerals 
Leases once a mineral deposit has been identified. Leases are granted for a renewable 
term of 15 years, and require annual payments of $3.50/ha for rent to maintain them in 
good standing. There are no work requirements for the maintenance of leases and they 
confer rights to minerals. 
 

The Permits are held 100% by DNI and are subject to a traditional royalty 
retained by the Province of Alberta against production revenues therefrom. The Alberta 
Metallic Mineral Royalty consists of two parts: (i) upon commencement of Production, a 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 12 
 

1% mine mouth royalty is levied on gross revenues, net of costs incurred between the 
mine mouth and the point of sale or the point at which fair value is determined, as 
prescribed by the Minister; and (ii) an additional 12% royalty levied on that portion of net 
operating revenues at the mine mouth which exceed costs incurred in the exploration, 
development, recovering, processing, transportation or disposition, and any other such 
cost allowances as specified by the Minister. In broad terms, the aggregate royalty is 
akin to a 1% traditional net smelter return royalty in addition to a 12% net profits royalty. 
The reader is referred to Alberta Mines and Minerals Act, Metallic and Industrial 
Minerals Royalty Regulation for details. 
 

The Permits grant DNI a right to use of the surface for the purposes of 
conducting mineral exploration work, subject to obtaining the necessary land use 
permits (Exploration Approval) from the Land Administration Division of Alberta 
Sustainable Resource Development (SRD). Surface restrictions can consist of minor 
activity restrictions, which are usually identified in any land use permit granted.  
 

There are no other surface encumbrances in the area, other than compensation, 
which may from time to time, be payable to compensate Forest Agreement Area (FMA) 
holders for timber rights over portions of the area, in the event timber is cleared during 
construction of drill roads and pads.  In addition, compensation may be payable from 
time to time for access through trappers permit areas. 
 

Complete terms and conditions for mineral exploration permitting and work can 
be found in the Mines and Minerals Act and Regulations contained within it (Metallic and 
Industrial Minerals Tenure Regulation 145/2005, Metallic and Industrial Minerals 
Exploration Regulation 213/98). This and additional Acts and Regulations, with respect 
to Mineral Exploration and Mining can be found in the Laws Online section of the 
Government of Alberta Queens Printer at www.qp.alberta.ca/Laws_Online.cfm. 
 
4.3 - COEXISTING OIL, GAS AND OIL SANDS RIGHTS 
 

Rights to metallic and industrial minerals, to bitumen (oil sands), to coal and to 
oil/gas within the region are regulated under separate statutes, which collectively make 
it possible for several different "rights" to coexist and be held by different grantees over 
the same geographic location. 
 

Coexistence of rights in the area is an artifact of the flat-lying configuration of 
subsurface geological formations within the region, and the potential of different 
formations for hosting different resources including oil, gas and minerals. Oil/gas leases, 
coal leases, oil sands leases and MAIM permits coexist in the Birch Mountains in the 
vicinity of, and under, DNI's Property. Existing oil/gas leases and oil sands permits in 
the vicinity of, and under, DNI's SBH Property.  A number of the key oil sands projects 
and properties are shown in Figures 4.2 and 4.3, respectively.  Large Oil Sands project 
near DNI's SBH Property include the Fort Hills oil sands mine (construction stage), the 
Equinox oil sands mine (planning stage), the Pierre River oil sands mine (planning 
stage), the Frontier oil sands (planning stage) and the Horizon oil sands mine 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 13

Figure 4.3: Key Oil Sands Properties and Projects near DNI's Permits (After Figure 4 in Sabag, 

2010). 

 
(construction stage). In December 2011, the Canadian government gave Total SA 
approval to start construction on its $CDN 9 billion Joslyn North oil sands project in 
Alberta, marking the fifth mining development in the vast crude deposit. 
 

Rights to oil sands in the area are confined to the Wabiskaw and McMurray 
formations (approximately 400 m beneath DNI's Second White Speck Shale targets), 
and include rights to metals accompanying the oil sands. Gas leases and oil sands 
permits over the Birch Mountains, under DNI's Property, relate to formations deeper 
within the stratigraphy well below the metal-bearing shale formations targeted by DNI. 
 
4.4 - PRIOR OWNERSHIP 
 

DNI acquired the current SBH Property Permits directly, by application to Alberta 
Energy, and holds a 100% interest therein under MAIM agreements with Alberta 
Energy.  All prior, historic mineral activities in the area consist entirely of grass roots 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 15 
 

exploration work. There are no historic mineral mines or resources that are known in the 
area or on the Property.  
 

DNI's Property encompasses several historic Property boundaries, previously 
held and explored for minerals by others. Prior historic ownership as well as historic 
exploration work and discoveries is well summarized by Sabag (2008). To maintain 
continuity with historic work, DNI has elected to retain historic location names to 
facilitate referencing of prior year results by referring to the historic Buckton, Asphalt 
and Eaglenest Property names, but as "Mineralized Zones" or "Sub-Properties" within 
the SBH Property.  
 
4.5 - LAND USE AND ENVIRONMENTAL MATTERS 
 

Land use in the area is regulated by the Lands Division of SRD, which regulates 
issuance of land use permits for surface disturbances, with participation from a 
structured local consultation process. For the 2011 drilling campaign, DNI conducted a 
variety of consultation meetings with various aboriginal groups in the Fort MacKay and 
Fort McMurray area in order to acquire the necessary land use permit "MME Exploration 
Approval" to conduct their drilling program.  DNI was successful in acquiring the 
required "MME Permit" to conduct its 2011 drilling program.  For future exploration 
activities, DNI will have to obtain the necessary permitting to conduct further drilling and 
other such work of an invasive nature which might disturb the surface (and will have to 
subsequently reclaim any disturbances made). DNI has submitted an application and is 
currently undergoing the approval process for a new MME land use permit in order to 
continue and complete the original proposed 2011 drilling program.  DNI has been 
informed by SRD that they will not be required to conduct further aboriginal consultation 
in for the new MME land use permit. 
 

Minor sensitivities exist in the region, which affect exploration activities and land 
use to an extent comparable to elsewhere in Canada. These include wolf migration, 
moose and caribou calving seasons, and trapping rights. Wood Buffalo National Park is 
located 10 km to the north of the northernmost boundary of the SBH Property and 
approximately 33 km north of the Buckton Mineralized Zone. There are no known 
aboriginal claims pending in the region.  
 

Surface restrictions consist of minor activity restrictions over portions of the 
Property as shown on Figure 4.4 and are as follows: (i) the surface over the western 
one third of DNI's Property is subject to seasonal activity restrictions in connection with 
caribou calving and migration routes requiring the annual recess of field activities during 
the four month period March 1 through July 1; (ii) The eastern portion of the Property 
now falls under the new Wildlife and Biodiversity Zones, which functions in the same 
manner as the Key Wildlife and Watercourse Areas, Ungulate Winter Areas or Moose 
zones, where timing restrictions for activity are between January 15

 

to April 30; (iii) a 
small acreage on Permit  9307110951 is set aside as a historic site over a portage to 
the south of Eaglenest Lake; and (iv) a small area on Permits 9308060411 and 
9308060413 is set aside under historic management.  Currently, there are no known  



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 16 
 

 
Figure 4.4: Summary Sketch of Activity Restrictions (after Figure 5 in Sabag, 2010).  

 
 
restrictions on or major obstacles to resource development for the SBH Property and for 
the region. 
 

Regional gas accumulations, occur in the southeast and southwestern portions of 
the region surrounding Fort McMurray. Low pressure gas has been documented from 
the Viking Formation known to occur at depths of 200-300 m beneath surface in the 
Birch Mountains under portions of DNI's Property. The Viking Formation is lower in the 
stratigraphy, and is deeper than DNI's targeted shales and has not been considered a 
hindrance to exploration. Higher pressure gas has been documented from deeper in the 
stratigraphy, from the McMurray Formation (host to Oil Sands) approximately 500-600 
m below the surface of the Birch Mountains. Scattered gas pockets are common 
throughout region, hence taking due precaution during drilling is common practice.  
 

Timber rights for a considerable portion of the region, including the Birch 
Mountains Area, are held by various groups under Provincial Forest Management 
Agreements. Rights in the Birch Mountains Area are held mainly by Alberta Pacific 
Forestry Industries Inc. (Al-Pac), the Crown and trappers, necessitating compensation 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 17 
 

payable by way of timber damage assessment (TDA) in the event any clearing is made 
during preparation of drill pads and access. TDA rates are applicable to all land 
clearing, regardless of quantity and quality of growth. For the SBH Property TDA 
payments range at approximately the $1,000 per ha of clearing, which includes $421/ha 
to the Crown and a small portion for trappers compensation. 
 
 
5. ACCESS, CLIMATE, PHYSIOGRAPHY AND LOCAL INFRASTRUCTURE 
 
5.1 - ACCESS AND INFRASTRUCTURE 
 

DNI's SBH Property is located approximately 120 km north of Fort McMurray and 
is accessible by highway from Edmonton (350 km away) and by regular daily 
commercial flights from Edmonton, Calgary and Toronto. Principal access is by road, 
though plans are underway by Canadian National Railway Company (CN Rail) to 
rehabilitate historic rail shipping service to Fort McMurray.  
 

The region is well supplied and offers all necessary support services to 
exploration work in the area, inclusive of expediting, fixed and rotary air support, 
communications, medical and equipment supplies. Radio as well as telephone 
communications are also excellent throughout the region. Cellular telephone coverage 
is good throughout the region, with good reception to localities as far away as the Birch 
Mountains air strip and fire tower. 
 

The Athabasca and the Clearwater Rivers represent the two principle waterways 
in the region, with countless other streams and smaller rivers draining into them. The 
majority of the streams are characterized by jagged shapes consisting of many 
relatively straight water courses, reflecting in most part underlying faults and joint 
systems. The Athabasca River bisects the region and provides relatively good water 
access across most of the region and also a barge service over its northern portions to 
the north of Fort McMurray. The Athabasca River flows north into Lake Athabasca. 
 

Access throughout the region is relatively good, facilitated by a network of 
highways, secondary roads and old seismic lines, which serve well as winter roads and 
bush roads, and in some cases are also accessible by all-terrain vehicles. Past 
exploration activities have occasionally gained access to the west shore of the 
Athabasca River by ice-bridge constructed from a locality near Bitumont, as a joint effort 
between forestry harvesting and mineral exploration. Future programs will, however, 
benefit from considerable road construction in progress to support several dozen 
pending oil sand operations, which are in various stages of development. 
 

Access throughout the east and west flanks of the Athabasca River are in a state 
of rapid development, providing road access to several pending oil sand projects 
skirting the Birch Mountains over localities adjacent to the south and east boundaries of 
DNI's Property, to as far north as its northeast corner (the Property is surrounded on its 
east and south by four oils sands mines under development). Significant pending 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 18 
 

developments include Shell Canada's planned construction of a bridge across the 
Athabasca River to access its Pierre River oil sands mine (permitting stage), adjacent to 
the east boundary of DNI's Property. This will significantly enhance access to the 
Property, since the planned Pierre River Mine is, downslope from the Buckton and 
Asphalt Mineralized Zones.  
 

The Birch Mountains have traditionally been accessed in the summer months by 
barge/boat via the Athabasca River, although the principal mode of access has been by 
rotary aircraft or by fixed wing aircraft landing on the half mile long Birch Mountain 
Airstrip which also houses a seasonally manned Fire Tower and telecommunications 
relay station. There are other private airstrips throughout the region, the nearest being 
Shell Canada's at its Pierre River Project, and Canadian Natural Resources Limited's 
(CNRL) Horizon Oil Sands Project to the south of the Property.  
 

Winter access is via the paved road that runs north from Fort MacKay to the 
Horizon Mine Site, then by winter road from the headwaters of the Tar River at the west 
edge of the Horizon Mine Site to a trail that runs west from the Birch Mountain Airstrip. 
This trail winds itself north and east along a plateau over-looking the Buckton 
Mineralized Zone. Winter road access was ploughed in down to the drill sites in early 
1997 and 2011 by the use of snow cats and a series of dozers. 
 
5.2 - PHYSIOGRAPHY, VEGETATION AND CLIMATE 
 

Physiography over the general region around Fort McMurray, is variable and is 
characterized by low, often swampy, relief punctuated by a handful of features 
protruding above the otherwise flat terrain. The Birch Mountains are one of the most 
conspicuous topographic features in the region and are located in the north of the 
region, to the south of Wood Buffalo National Park. DNI's Property covers a large 
portion of the Birch Mountains. 
 

By far the greatest topographic relief in the region are the Birch Mountains (750-
820 m elevation above sea level (asl)) which protrude conspicuously some 500-600 m 
above the surrounding areas (250 m asl), with a distinct sharp erosional edge. The 
Birch Mountains are characterized by many river and creek incisions in poorly 
consolidated stratigraphy susceptible to active landslides and slumps. River valley 
incisions in the area are progressively deeper as they near the erosional edges of the 
Birch Mountains and the drainage in the area defines an approximate radial pattern 
outward from the Birch Mountains. 
 

Given the relatively flat lying stratigraphy in the region, the Birch Mountains 
provide excellent vertical exposures, especially in river valleys, across relatively long 
sections of nearly flat lying stratigraphy, which are otherwise buried to the west and 
eroded to the south and east. One of the most prominent topographic features in the 
Birch Mountains area is the McIvor River valley representing a 20 km long east-
northeasterly trending valley which opens to a width of some 10 km at its eastern 
extremity. Unlike other sharply incised valleys in the Birch Mountains area, it is a 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 19 
 

relatively flat-bottomed feature dominated by the McIvor River with its many braided 
meanders and countless tributaries. The valley is surrounded by zones of active slump 
representing broad zones of continual sediment recharge such that the active flow 
channel of the McIvor River is in a continual state of flux within the central section of the 
river valley, shifting back and forth within several hundred meters of the valley bottom. 
The headwaters of the McIvor River, which are on DNI's SBH Property, flow north into 
Lake Claire and Wood Buffalo Park. 
 

Northeast Alberta weather is somewhat cooler than the central Canadian 
provinces. Winter temperatures are cold, averaging -20 degrees Celsius (°C) (min -
40°C), and summers are warm averaging 17°C (max 30°C), and are typically short 
(June to August) much like northern Canada. Average annual precipitation for Fort 
McMurray is approximately 460 millimetres (mm). The Birch Mountains, by virtue of 
elevation, are somewhat cooler than rest of the region, and are susceptible to fog during 
long periods of wet weather. 
 

Glacial history of the region is complex and not clearly understood. Principal ice 
direction throughout the northeastern portion of the region is southwesterly, although ice 
flow is believed to have splayed around (and over) the Birch Mountains such that 
throughout the balance of the region there is evidence of crosscutting composite 
directions, manifested as multiple till sheets and fluted topography. The Alberta 
Geological Survey (AGS) has an ongoing geoscience program dedicated to the 
investigation and mapping of Northern Alberta's Quaternary geology. 
 
 
6. HISTORY 
 
6.1 - PRIOR OWNERSHIP HISTORY - HISTORIC PROPERTIES 
 

A significant amount of mineral exploration was conducted in the Birch Mountains 
by a number of companies during the 1990's including and most prominently Tintina 
Mines Ltd. (Tintina).  Tintina discovered the poly-metallic Second White Specks black 
shale by accident in 1995 while searching for metal bearing permeability traps in redox 
fronts across northeast Alberta. Two of the authors of this Technical Report, Mr. 
Dufresne and Mr. Eccles, were involved in much of the ongoing exploration for poly-
metallic zones in the Birch Mountains conducted in the 1990's.  The historic exploration 
is briefly summarized below and is provided in detail by Mr. Sabag in the following 
reports (Sabag, 2010, 2008, 1999, 1998, 1996a, 1996b and 1996c).  In particular, the 
reader is referred to DNI's most recent assessment report (Sabag, 2010) and Technical 
Report (Sabag, 2008) for excellent summaries of the historic exploration. 
 

DNI's SBH Property encompasses the historic Buckton, Asphalt and Eaglenest 
Properties, which were previously held by, and extensively explored by Tintina during 
the 1990's. The Property also contains a single township permit previously held by Ells 
River Resources, and the southern parts of an early-stage Property previously held by 
Ultrasonic Industries. Historic property boundaries including those permits formerly held 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 20 
 

by Tinitan, Ells River Resources and Ultrasonic Industries along with select significant 
results are illustrated in comparison to DNI's current land holdings in Figure 6.1. 
 

Geological databases from historic work conducted over and surrounding the 
above historic Properties, together with work conducted by the AGS and GSC are 
unique and in the possession of DNI. The Buckton, Asphalt and Eaglenest historic 
Properties are now referred to as Mineralized Zones. 
 
6.2 - PREVIOUS WORK HISTORY – METAL EXPLORATION 
 

The area under DNI's MAIM Permits and the broader Birch Mountains Area, was 
aggressively explored during the period 1993-1999 by Tintina as part of its exploration 
programs over a much larger (3 million acre) land position it then held across northeast 
Alberta. Tintina's 1990's work spanned the full spectrum of exploration activities ranging 
from grass roots reconnaissance and systematic regional sampling (1994-1995), 
through in-fill sampling, anomaly identification and follow-up (1995-1997), to exploration 
drilling (1996-1997) and preliminary metallurgical test work, leaching and bench tests 
(1997-1999). Diamond indicator investigations and extensive check assaying work 
(1997-1999) were also completed. For additional work on Tintina's exploration 
programs, the reader is referred to Sabag (2010; 2008; 1998). A general summary is 
included in the Buckton inferred maiden resource estimate (Dufresne et al., 2011). For 
additional information on the Diamond potential for the Birch Mountains and surrounding 
area, the reader is referred to Dufresne et al. (1994), Sabag (2010, 2008) and Eccles 
(2011). 
 
6.3 - DNI WORK HISTORY 
 
6.3.1 – Discovery and 1997 Drilling  
 

Tintina discovered the poly-metallic Second White Specks black shale by 
accident in 1995 while conducting other exploration in the birch Mountains and across 
northeast Alberta (Sabag, 2010 and 2008). Exploration work performed by DNI during 
2008 to 2010 consisted of a variety of programs including the following: (i) regional and 
property scale geological data synthesis and compilation, including synthesis of 
information from the Western Canada Sedimentary Basin with specific focus on 
northeast Alberta and the Birch Mountains area (2007- 2009); (ii) Consolidation of the 
historic information from geological data synthesis and compilation into databases as 
well as preparation of a NI-43-101 compliant Technical Report for the Property (Sabag, 
2008); (iii) review, inventory and verification analysis of historic third-party (Tintina) drill 
core archived at the MCRF from the Property (2008-2009); (iv) Expansion of the 
subsurface geological database, related synthesis and subsurface stratigraphic 
modelling (2008-2010); (v) Strategic field sampling program and related analytical work 
(2009 and 2010); (vi) A number of leaching and mineral studies as follows: Initial 
cyanidation leaching test work (2009); micro-scaled mineral (MLA) study (2009-2010); 
bio-organism cultivation, culture adaptation and bioleaching studies (BRGM and ARC, 
2009-2010), sulphuric acid leaching test work (2010); (vii) a CO2 Sequestration study – 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 21 
 

ARC (2009-2010), and (viii) a drilling program and resource estimates for primary 
metals such as Mo, Ni, U, V, Zn, Cu, Co and Li (Dufresne et al., 2011) and for REE, the 
latter of which is the subject of this Technical Report. APEX personnel were involved 
with several of the above studies between 2008 and 2011. 
 

For a full review of historical exploration work the reader is referred to the 
Buckton inferred maiden resource estimate (Dufresne et al., 2011) and more complete 
accounts by Sabag (2010; 2008; 1998). Some of the historical details pertinent to the 
discovery of the Buckton Mineralized Zone and the REE-Y-Sc-Th resource estimate are 
re-iterated in the following text.  
 

In a NI 43-101 Technical Report, Sabag (2008) suggested that multiple poly-
metallic zones in the Second White Specks Shale exist over vast areas (50-100 km

2
 

each), occurring as flat-lying near-surface "blankets" amenable to extraction by open pit 
bulk mining methods. Sabag (2008) indicates that several potential poly-metallic zones 
were identified by historic work, two of which were confirmed by historic drilling. One 
such poly-metallic zone is the Buckton Zone which is the focus of this Technical Report. 
Sabag (2010 and 2008) describes the Buckton Mineralized Zone as a Potential Mineral 
Deposit that represents a near-surface poly-metallic enrichment zone in the Second 
White Speckled Shale.  
 

The Buckton Mineralized Zone was discovered in 1997 when six vertical core 
holes were drilled along an 8 km transect generally paralleling intermittent exposures of 
the Second White Speckled Shale along the adjacent valley walls of Gos Creek on the 
eastern flanks of the Birch Mountains in northeastern Alberta. Relying on the historic 
1997 drilling results and select bedrock exposures of the Second White Speckled Shale 
Formation in incised valley's, DNI's reported that the Buckton Zone contains a 
conceptual Mo-Ni-U-V-Zn-Cu-Co-Li deposit over an area of approximately 26 km

2
. 

Sabag (2010 and 2008) indicates that the Buckton Potential Mineral Deposit has good 
lateral continuity and is vertically zoned, containing generally better grading poly-
metallic material over its upper half, and progressively better grades northward in the 
upper parts of the drill holes accompanied by progressive northward thickening of the 
better grading sections. The prospective Second White Specks Shale is approximately 
21 m in thickness and represents an aggregate of approximately 1.1-1.2 billion tonnes 
of mineralized material (1.2 to 1.3 billion short tons; Sabag, 2010 and 2008). Sabag 
(2010 and 2008) indicates that the Buckton Potential Mineral Deposit is open to the 
south, the west and the north. Its projected northerly extension holds the best potential 
for providing considerable additional mineralized volumes over an additional 5 to 10 km 
under sufficiently thin overburden cover to have realistic potential for access by open 
pit.  
 
6.3.2 - 2009 to 2010 Leaching Test Work 
 

In 2009 DNI commenced an extensive metallurgical test work program focused 
on acid and bioleaching studies in order to assess the recoverability of metals from the 
Second White Specks Shale using various leaching techniques.  Samples were 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 22 
 

collected by APEX staff and DNI from outcrops of the Second White Specks Shale 
during 2009 and 2010.  These samples and composites of the samples were then used 
in a variety of leaching test programs at several different facilities using a number of 
techniques. The procedures and results are well summarized by Sabag (2010) and in 
DNI corporate news releases between 2010 and 2011 (DNI News Releases dated 
March 4, 2010; April 28, 2010 and September 8, 2011).  
 

From 2009 to 2010, leaching test work was conducted at the Bureau de 
Recherches Géologiques et Minières (BRGM), France, Alberta Innovates Technology 
Futures (AITF and formerly the Alberta Research Council), Edmonton, Alberta, and 
Actlabs, Ancaster, Ontario. In general, the test work indicates that most of the metals 
that are of interest in the Second White Specks Shale can be extracted with reasonable 
recoveries. 
 

The Sulfuric acid leaching tests performed at Actlabs successfully demonstrated 
that: (i) collective group of metals can indeed be extracted from the shale by simple 
leaching under conditions generally simulating bioleaching; (ii) that high recoveries can 
be achieved under conditions for Ni-U-Zn-Cd-Co, and middling recoveries for Cu-Li; (iii) 
that recoveries for Mo-V are poor, but can be enhanced by varying leaching 
parameters; (iv) that rare metals contained in the shale, including Li, also report as co-
products during leaching and that they represent previously unrecognized additional 
value to the shale; and (v) that the Second Whites Specks Shale is likely amenable to 
bioleaching, provided the shale contains bio-organisms suitable for bioleaching and 
barring any toxicity presented to bio-cultures by the geochemistry of the shale. 
 

Sabag (2010) concluded that the bioleaching tests conducted by the BRGM in 
France and by AITF in Edmonton, Alberta indicate: (i) bioculturing and adaptation test 
work demonstrated that microorganisms capable of growing under bioleaching 
conditions naturally exist in the Second White Specks Shale and that enrichment 
cultures can be obtained from the shale whose adaptation to the Shale is immediate, 
and that there is no "poisoning" by the shale's geochemistry nor does the shale 
chemistry inhibit start-up of bacterial growth; (ii) bioleaching tests of fresh surface 
samples from the Asphalt and Buckton Zones indicate that the shale is amenable to 
bioleaching; (iii) batch amenability bioleaching tests demonstrated that a collective 
group of metals can be extracted (recovered) from the shale and that non-optimized 
high recoveries typically ranging 80%-95% can be achieved for Ni-U-Zn-Cd-Co, 
middling recoveries typically ranging 40%-55% can be achieved for Cu-Li; and typically 
poor recoveries documented for Mo-V, ranging 2%-50% for Mo and 2%-30% for V, 
might be partly due to re-precipitation of Mo and V from solution associated with re-
precipitation of Fe and sulphates, and (iv) Mo and V recoveries might be enhanced by 
optimization of the leaching parameters. 
 

Further details of the leaching test work, including rare metals leaching test work, 
are summarized in Section 13 below. 
 
 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 23 
 

7. GEOLOGICAL SETTING AND MINERALIZATION 
 
7.1 – INTRODUCTION AND GENERAL GEOLOGICAL SETTING 
 

Alberta is mostly underlain by sedimentary sequences of the Western Canada 
Sedimentary Basin (WCSB). The WCSB is bounded by the Canadian Shield to the east 
and by the thrust-fold foothills and the Rocky Mountains to the west. A relatively stable 
Precambrian basement with localized reactivation zones underlies the WCSB. Regional 
stratigraphy is mostly known from oil well formation picks, due to the scarcity of outcrop 
everywhere except along river valleys. A stratigraphic column for the region is shown in 
Figure 7.1. The WCSB consists of a wedge of flat-lying Devonian sediments 
(carbonates, evaporites and clastic red beds) overlain by equally flat-lying Cretaceous 
and Cenozoic clastic sediments that thicken to over 7,000 m in southwestern Alberta 
(Figure 7.2).  
 

Precambrian rocks underlying the region belong to the Talston Magmatic Arc 
(TMA) and the Rae Province.  The TMA is a major crustal suture zone marking the 
boundary between the Archean Rae Province to the east and the Proterozoic Buffalo 
Head Terrain to the west (Ross and Bowring, 1991), and it is characterized by a sinuous 
aeromagnetic fabric consistent with the geology of its exposed portions in the northeast 
of the region where large anastomosing mylonitic shear zones cut through large (up to 
50 km diameter) granitic batholiths intruding 2.0-1.8 billion year (Ga) old ortho- and 
paragneisses. The TMA can be traced north for several hundred km from the Snowbird 
Tectonic Zone (approximately 100 km southeast of Fort McMurray) to the Great Slave 
Lake Shear Zone where it is displaced to the northeast and continues as the Thelon 
Magmatic Zone. 
 

The near-surface geology consists of Middle Cretaceous Colorado Group 
sedimentary rocks that comprise the Westgate, Fish Scale, Belle Fourche, Second 
White Specks, and LaBiche formations. The various members of the Colorado Group 
represent depositional events that extended over much of North America over a period 
of 25-30 million years, at a time when sea levels were high and the North American 
craton was experiencing a regional downwarping (Leckie et al, 1992). As a result, the 
Colorado Group is dominated by marine shales which are occasionally punctuated by 
coarser sediments deposited during brief high-stands. The Colorado Group reaches a 
maximum thickness of approximately 1,500 m in northwest Alberta and is generally 
thickest nearer the Cordillera. The erosional edge of the Colorado Group in northeast 
Alberta is represented by a shale dominated package of strata which reaches a 
maximum thickness of approximately 450 – 500 m in the Birch Mountains (the Colorado 
Group underlies all of DNI's Properties in the Birch Mountains, and dominates near 
surface exposures). 
 

The Colorado Group includes two distinctive basin-wide stratigraphic markers, 
the Fish Scales Zone and the Second White Specks Zone. The Fish Scales Zone is 
located in the central part of the Shaftesbury Formation and consists of a concentration 
of fish debris, such as bones, teeth and scales, within shales (and minor sandstone) 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 24 
 

with relatively high total organic carbon values of 5-10% (Bloch et al 1993). The 
Formation is generally less than 20 m thick, and can contain >75% fish debris. It may 
represent either an anoxic event at the Albian-Cenomanian boundary which prevented 
the normal decay of the bioclastic material or as a transgressive lag deposit. It is poorly 
understood and delineated and is normally characterized as a fish scales-bearing 
mudstone with minor associated sandstones and conglomerates, with up to 8% organic 
carbon (Bloch et al 1993).

Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 26 
 

The Belle Fourche Member of the Shaftesbury Formation overlies the Fish 
Scales Zone, and consists of massive mudstones characterized by low amounts of total 
organic carbon. A distinctive foraminiferal assemblage and a lack of bioclastic material 
distinguish it from the underlying Fish Scale Zone and the overlying Second White 
Specks Shale (Bloch et al, 1993). The Belle Fourche Formation is not well exposed in 
the region with the exception of many slump zones throughout the Birch Mountains 
which contain masses of gray shales and mudstones.  
 

The Second White Speckled Shale (or Second White Specks Formation) is 
named for the common occurrence of coccoliths. Black shale, which dominates this 
interval, is characterized by elevated total organic Carbon content, exceeding 10% by 
weight. The Second White Specks shale comprises a 'cherty' (?) bioclastic sandstone 
layer, referred to as the Siliciclastic Bone Bed or SBB (thus differentiating it from the 
Fish Scales Marker Bone bed - FSMB), ranges in thickness from a few centimeters up 
to 1.2 m, and is normally calcite cemented. Just above the SBB there is usually a thin 
(approximately 10 cm) limestone or carbonate cemented siltstone bed overlain by a 5-
10 m interval marked by numerous thin (1-20 cm) bentonite seams. 
 

The upper LaBiche Formation, overlying the Second White Speckled Shale is 
poorly studied given lack of exposures in the area. Two small and badly slumping 
outcrops of massive gray Colorado or upper LaBiche Formation shale previously 
observed well above those of the Second White Specks Formation have been assumed 
to represent the youngest Cretaceous strata preserved in the Birch Mountains area of 
northeast Alberta. Micropaleontological examination of LaBiche Formation from Birch 
Mountains drilling at the Buckton Zone suggests an unexpected 4-6 million year gap 
between the top of the Second White Specks Formation and the base of the Colorado 
Formation / upper LaBiche shales, and indicates that shales previously logged/mapped 
as simply as LaBiche are likely part of the Upper Cretaceous Lea Park Formation. 
 

Structural elements in northeast Alberta are include regional and localized 
features, many of which occur within the Precambrian basement, but some of which are 
confined to the overlying stratigraphic sequence, in some cases, specifically to 
Devonian strata. By far the largest zone of disturbance in the region is the Peace River 
Arch, which trends across northeastern Alberta within a wide zone passing to the north 
of Fort MacKay, across the southern parts of the Birch Mountains. It comprises a 140 
km wide zone of structural disturbances, which were active from as early as the Late 
Paleozoic to the Late Cretaceous, with no readily discernible aeromagnetic or gravity 
expression, although a subtle crustal uplift at the Mohorovicic Discontinuity.  
 

Younger structures in the area, apparently restricted to the WCSB strata, are 
dominated by a series of regional northeasterly trending faults, several of which pass 
through the Fort MacKay area and vicinity (Figure 7.3). The principal member of this 
family of faults is a dextral strike-slip fault that is known from stratigraphic correlation of 
oil/gas well data (Martin and Jamin, 1963). Other members of the northeasterly group of 
linear trends are interpreted from broad surficial features and major offsets in regional 
aeromagnetic data, which have not been corroborated by stratigraphic correlations 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 27 
 

(Figure 7.3). It is worth noting however, that all metallic occurrences reported to date 
from the region are from locations at, or in the immediate vicinity of, northeasterly-
trending features, particularly where these features cross certain other Precambrian 
trends, or where they intersect the Prairie Evaporite Dissolution front (scarp; see 
Dufresne et al., 2011). The limited drilling penetrating the Precambrian suggests that at 
least some of the northeasterly structures noted in the sedimentary rocks reflect 
Precambrian features, and that offsets along the structures also include a substantive 
vertical component defining a complex horst/graben framework. 
 
7.2 PROPERTY GEOLOGY 
 

The Property covers the eastern half of the Birch Mountains including its eastern 
and southern erosional edges. Bedrock exposures throughout the Birch Mountains are 
scarce (<2% of surface area) and, given the flat-lying stratigraphy, are restricted to 
incised valley walls of the many creeks and rivers along the eastern and southeastern 
erosional edges of the Birch Mountains (Figure 7.4).  
 

The available exposures throughout the area, nonetheless, enable intermittent 
observation and sampling across 300-350 m of Cretaceous stratigraphy, extending well 
into the Colorado Group, straddling the Albian-Cenomanian boundary. Selected 
formations which have been mapped and sampled in historic work over the Birch 
Mountains are described below, capturing information from a large area extending north 
from the vicinity of Pierre River, through Asphalt Creek, across the Buckton Creek area 
to the McIvor River and its tributaries located immediately to the north of the Property. 
 

The full extent of the Westgate Formation is exposed in the Greystone-B section, 
north of McIvor River (Figure 7.4), as a massive (20m) poorly consolidated dark gray 
mudstone. The mudstones are interbedded with thin (<1cm) discontinuous (10-20 cm 
long) fine-grained sandstone and siltstone lenses within their uppermost 5m, and the 
top of the Formation is marked only by the sudden appearance of fish scales (i.e., base 
of the Fish Scale Zone). Westgate mudstones are frequently iron and sulfur stained, 
and yellowish sulfates (possibly jarosite) can be seen near the base of the formation at 
the Greystone-B lithosection in abundant irregular 2-4 m long and 1-3 cm wide 
fractures. The Westgate Formation is characterized by relatively subdued geochemical 
variations of V (average 115 ppm), Zn contents (average 89 ppm), Ni (average 27 ppm) 
and only sporadic gold and platinum group elements. 
 

Exposures of the Fish Scales Zone are rare in the area and have been positively 
identified only at Greystone-B, although other occurrences have also been noted in 
badly slumped exposures along Asphalt Creek. At Asphalt-F (Figure 7.4), friable float 
slabs and blocks up to 5 cm thick, composed of a concentrated bed of fish scales 
(>80% by volume) contain up to 5% P; 16% Fe; by slightly elevated base metal 
concentrations; by elevated Pt, Pd, Mo, As and Sb; and 20ppb and 17ppb Au. In 
addition, geochemically significant anomalies from the Fish Scales Zone have been 
identified at the Greystone-C exposure including up to 10.5% organic carbon, 117 ppm 
Cu, 228 ppm Ni, 942 ppm V, 761 ppm Zn, and 12 ppb Au. 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 28
