FIGURE 7.4: Property Outcrops and Lithosections (After Figure 36 in Sabag, 2010). 

 
The Second White Specks Formation has been mapped and sampled at 

exposures between the 600 m and 650 elevations (asl) along Mid, Asphalt, Gos, 
Greystone, and Current Creeks. The SBB attains a thickness exceeding 1 m at the Gos-
C lithosection near the Buckton Zone, where it is also associated with metal-enrichment 
in surrounding shales (Gos-C is at the eastern flank of a principal stratigraphic 
disturbance in the area). The SBB has been documented in the area from several 
elevations varying 600-640 m (a.s.l.), and that the variations are probably the result of 
multiple slumping events. However, repetitive sedimentary/extinction events cannot, be 
entirely ruled out. 
 

Samples of the Second White Speckls Formation have returned by far the most 
anomalous concentrations of base as well as precious metals from the Birch Mountains, 
in addition to yielding native Au grains from certain localities. Geochemical anomalies 
identified from the formation define relatively systematic base metal enrichment zones, 
dominated by Ni-Cu-Mo-V-Zn (±U-Co-Cd-Ag-Au), associated vertically with the more 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 30 
 

carbonaceous sections immediately overlying the SBB, and laterally with certain faults 
in the Birch Mountains. Asphalt-H, GOS1 and Gos-C present by far the best metal 
enrichment localities documented from the Second White Specks Formation in the area. 
 
7.3 – GEOLOGICAL INTERPRETATION OF THE HIGH RARE EARTH ELEMENT 
STRATIGRAPHIC ZONE 
 

Polymetallic-enrichment is so characteristic of the Second White Speckled Shale 
in comparison to its surrounding sedimentary rock units that geochemistry can 
effectively map its upper and lower boundaries (Figure 7.5). Despite this anomalous 
geochemical signature, there is some variation in the metal contents within the Second 
White Speckled Shale. Most of the main metals of interest (e.g., Mo, Ni, U and V) 
dominate the middle to uppermost portion of the Second White Speckled Shale. 
Interestingly, an REE-rich portion of the Second White Speckled Shale, which is the 
focus of this report, does not correlate well with most of the metals. Rather, the REE-
rich Second White Speckled Shale is located in the lower portion of the formation and 
subsequently does not correlate well with the units' upper section containing enriched 
values of Mo, Ni, V, Zn, Cu and Co (e.g., Ce versus Mo from drillhole 11BK-02; Figure 
7.5).  
 

An explanation as to why the REEs and the main upper polymetallic sections do 
not correlate is investigated by studying the REE's, which are powerful tracers in the 
study of evolution of geochemical systems. Rare earth elements exist in the trivalent 
state under most natural conditions and behave in a chemically coherent manner as a 
group. The exceptions are Ce and Eu, which behave anomalously under certain redox 
conditions and temperatures. Thus the presence or absence of Ce and Eu anomalies in 
hydrothermal sedimentary rocks provides clues to the physiochemical history of the 
fluids from which they precipitated (Peter, 2003). Europium transition from the 2+ to 3+ 
ion state is strongly dependent on temperature and to a lesser degree pH. Europium 
exists primarily as Eu

2+
 under high temperatures (>250ºC) and reducing conditions. 

Because other REE exist in the 3+ ion state (except Ce), the presence of Eu in the +2 
state results in a positive Eu anomaly in both the fluids and precipitates. However, if the 
hydrothermal fluids are low temperature and were never subjected to high temperatures 
in the subsurface, these fluids transport Eu as Eu

3+
 and REE are precipitated without 

discernible fractionation from each other. Cerium can occur in nature as a 3+ or 4+ ion 
such that the Ce anomaly is used to determine the redox state. Negative Ce anomalies 
characterize modern oxic seawater where Ce

3+
 is oxidized by atmospheric oxygen to 

Ce
4+

. Under oxidizing conditions, Ce
4+

 is insoluble and precipitates as colloidal 
hydroxide, or is adsorbed as CeO2 on surfaces of grains in bottom sediments.  
 

Chondrite and shale-normalized REE profiles are presented as individual sample 
values from the Second White Speckled Shale Formation (both high and low-medium 
REE enrichment) together with over-and under-lying shale units of the Labiche and 
Belle Fourche formations, respectively (Figures 7.6 and 7.7). Both normalization profiles 

clearly show the higher overall abundance of total REE (La-Lu,Y) in the highly REE-

enriched zone of the Second White Speckled Shale Formation. The average La-Lu,Y  



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 31 
 

Figure 7.5: Vertical profiles of selected elements from drillhole 11BK-02. The boundaries of the Second White Speckled Shale Formation is 
highlighted by the red dashed lines. Approximate boundaries of the Second White Speckled Shale with rare earth element enrichment is 
highlighted by the solid red block on the right side of the profile.  

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 32 
 

of REE-enriched Second White Speckled Shale Formation zone is 395 ppm versus the 
all other shale (186 to 228 ppm). 
 

Both normalization profiles show a distinctly more negative Ce anomaly in the 
Second White Speckled Shale Formation, particularly within the highly REE-enriched 
zone, in comparison to the surrounding Labiche and Belle Fourche shale (Figures 7.6 

and 7.7). This correlation is better shown in the diagram of La-Lu,Y versus Ce/Ce* 
where increasing REE,Y have a negative trending association with the Ce anomaly 
(Figure 7.8). This suggests that the high REE-enriched Second White Speckled Shale 
zone was deposited in a more oxidized redox state relative to the less REE-enriched 
portion of the Second White Speckled Shale and surrounding shale units. The drill core 
observations support this observation. Silty shale to siltstone characterize the high REE-
enriched Second White Speckled Shale zone in contrast to the black 'poker-chip', which 
dominates the rest of the Second White Speckled Shale Formation and is characteristic 
of deep marine shale.  
 

 
Figure 7.6: Chondrite-normalized rare earth element distribution patterns for middle to Late 
Cretaceous shale in the DNI Metals Inc. permit area. A) Second White Speckled Shale Formation zone 
with highest rare earth enrichment. B) Second White Speckled Shale Formation with elevated, but lower 
rare earth enrichment. C) Labiche Formation, which resides directly above the Second White Speckled 
Shale Formation. D) Belle Fourche Member of the Shaftesbury Formation, which resides directly below 
the Second White Speckled Shale Formation. Shaded polygon in B, C and D represents the Second 
White Speckled Shale zone with highest rare earth enrichment for comparison. Chondrite normalization 
values of Sun and McDonough  (1989).  
 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 33 
 

 
Figure 7.7: Shale-normalized rare earth element distribution patterns for middle to Late 
Cretaceous shale in the DNI Metals Inc. permit area. A) Second White Speckled Shale Formation zone 
with highest rare earth enrichment. B) Second White Speckled Shale Formation with elevated, but lower 
rare earth enrichment. C) Labiche Formation, which resides directly above the Second White Speckled 
Shale Formation. D) Belle Fourche Member of the Shaftesbury Formation, which resides directly below 
the Second White Speckled Shale Formation. Shaded polygon in B, C and D represents the Second 
White Speckled Shale zone with highest rare earth enrichment for comparison. Post-Archean Australian 
Shale (PAAS) normalization values of Taylor and McLennan (1985). 

 
 
The chondrite-normalized REE profiles off all the shales exhibit comparable 

negative Eu anomalies (Figure 7.6). To reiterate, a positive Eu anomaly is typical of 
high-temperature hydrothermal fluids, but low temperature fluids such as those from the 
Teahitia submarine volcano (30ºC; Michard et al., 1993) produce trivalent Eu yielding no 
Eu anomaly. However, variations in the REE, and particularly the Eu and middle REE 
(MREE), are better emphasized by normalizing the REE to post-Archean shale standard 
(PAAS; Figure 7.7). The Labiche and the majority of the Belle Fourche shale units have 
a generally flat REE profile. In contrast, the Second White Speckled Shale and a few 
samples of Belle Fourche, yield an arching positive MREE profile relative to Ce and the 
heavy REE (HREE). If the depositional environment did become more oxygenated as 
suggested earlier by interpretation of the Ce anomaly, then a plausible explanation for 
the MREE-enriched patterns involves adsorption of REE by iron oxyhydroxides, which is 
similar to low-grade, MREE-enriched REE ion-adsorption clay deposits in southern 
China (see Section 8. Deposit Types).  
 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 34 
 

 
Figure 7.8: Sum of the lanthanides plus yttrium versus the cerium anomaly. 

 
 
To expand on the idea of REE being scavenged by ironhydroxides, we 

considered other elements that correlate well with the high REE-enriched Second White 
Speckled Shale section. Predominantly, these include phosphate and sulfur (Figure 
7.5). Phosphate was previously thought to be directly associated with fish debris in 
numerous bone beds layers occurring at the same stratigraphic horizon as the REE-
enriched Second White Speckled Shale section (Dufresne et al., 2001). These authors 
also reported that the bone beds yield a more pronounced negative Ce anomaly than 
the shale and that the bone beds often contain sulphide.  
 

However, the bone beds are unusual in that there often is a noticeable lack of 
bone material and the 'bone beds' layers comprise black waxy looking nodules up to 1 
mm in diameter. A more plausible, but yet to be proven explanation, is that these layers 
comprise dissolved and/or particulate P, which is known to be readily scavenged from 
the ambient hydrothermal plume onto oxyhydroxide plume particulates that eventually 
settle on the seafloor (e.g., P nodules associated with hydrothermal mounds of the Juan 

de Fuca Ridge; Krasukopf, 1979). The positive correlation between La-Lu,Y and P/Al, 
in combination of the negative Ce anomaly (oxidizing conditions) and sulphide, provide 
noteworthy evidence that precipitation of oxyhydroxides likely occur within the REE-
enriched layers (Figure 7.9). Precipitation of oxyhydroxides is supported by scanning 
electron microprobe test work on the shale, which suggests that metals occur as 
charged particles within oxides, hydroxides and clays, rather than as discrete mineral 
phases (Hamilton, 2010; Sabag, 2010). 
 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 35 
 

 

 
Figure 7.9: Sum of the lanthanides plus yttrium versus phosphorus/aluminum. 

 
 

This theory explains why the REE-rich Second White Speckled Shale does not 
correspond stratigraphically with the polymetallic-enriched zones. While the 
hydrothermal activity is presumed to be active throughout the Second White Speckled 
Shale, the lower to middle portion of the unit was subject to higher oxidation levels in 
comparison to the rest of the formation, which is characterized as dominantly black 
deep marine shale. The more oxic environment created the perfect environment for 
precipitation of oxyhydroxides that then scavenged REE, P and S from the buoyant 
plume. The plume particulates eventually settle to the seafloor where they are 
incorporated into the metalliferous sediment. In contrast, the base-metal rich 
polymetallic layers of the Second White Speckled Shale are deposited directly onto the 
seafloor in an anoxic reducing 'black shale' environment with limited scavenging. Fluids 
in both anoxic and oxygenated settings are relatively cool and never exceeded or 
interacted with temperatures exceeding 250ºC. 
 
 
8. DEPOSIT TYPES 
 
8.1 BLACK SHALE DEPOSITS 
  

Black shales worldwide represent important hosts for the concentration of 
immense metallic mineral resources, especially for precious and transitional metals and 
Uranium. Black shales are generally regarded to have been deposited within anoxic 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 36 
 

deep water depositional environments, although they can be formed in a broad variety 
of depositional environments ranging from fresh to estuarine to marine waters with 
conditions ranging from anoxic to oxic (Quinby-Hunt and Wilde, 1996).The Second 
White Speckled Shale Formation is classified as a "metal enriched black shale" because 
of its: (i) relatively thick tabular geometry of the metalliferous black shale layers 
alternating with layers of ejecta material (bentonites and pyroclastic material); (ii) 
diagnostic characteristic Ni/Cu ratios; (iii) spatial association of metal enrichment zones 
with suspected venting (volcanic centers); (iv) predominance of V-Zn-Cu mineralization 
over Ni-Mo-PGE (based on relative grades).  
 

The principal metals of interest in the Zones are Mo, Ni, U, V, Zn, Cu, Co, and 
rare metals, including Li, REE, Y and Sc as potential incidental co-products of 
significant value recoverable from the black shale. Though none of the metals is present 
in sufficient quantity in the Second White Specks Shale to be considered the "pay" 
metal by itself and leading the anticipated value of any deposit identified, the collective 
metals on a combined basis provide a significant economic value if they can be 
effectively recovered from the host rock on a combined basis such as in situ or pad 
leaching might provide.  
 

Few black shale ores have been commercially exploited on a large scale, though 
many have been sporadically mined on a local scale and are associated with other 
deposits or mining camps often with an affinity to large metal-bearing geological 
systems. The Uraniferous Alum Shales, Sweden, and the poly-metallic Talvivaara black 
shale hosted deposit, Finland, provide examples of currently active black shale 
exploration and development operations, the latter of which commenced production in 
October 2008. Talvivaara expects to reach full capacity production by the end of 2012.  
The company reports that is has paid back its initial capital construction loan early and 
that ramp up of bioheapleaching is ahead of schedule with performance above 
budgeted levels. Currently, Talvivaara is nearing a cash positive operation and is 
performing beyond expectations. Talvivaara is predominantly a Ni-Zn-rich black shale 
deposit with credits of Cu, Co and U. 
 
 
8.2 WORLDWIDE RARE EARTH ELEMENT DEPOSIT TYPES 
 

Rare earth element mineral species are divided into silicate (e.g., allanite), oxide 
(e.g., fergusonite), carbonate (e.g., bastnaesite, parisite, synchysite, ancylite), and 
phosphate (monazite, rhapdophane, xenotime, britholite, apatite), and are found in both 
primary and secondary residual deposits. Potential economic and producing REE 
deposit types include carbonatite, hydrothermal/magmatic Fe-REE, placer, alkaline- and 
peralkaline-related, and ion-adsorption deposits.  

 
 Representative examples of carbonatite intrusions enriched in REE include 
Mountain Pass, California, USA and Bayan Obo in Inner Mongolia, China. Carbonatite 
occurs both in relatively stable intra-plate and near plate margins, the latter potentially 
related to orogenic activity. Original carbonatite magma may form either by partial 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 37 
 

melting of carbonate-bearing mantle without significant assimilation of crustal material, 
or through fractionation and/or liquid immiscibility between carbonate and silicate melts 
(Mitchell, 2005).  
 
 Iron-REE deposits include orthomagmatic magnetite-apatite and hydrothermal 
iron-oxide-copper-gold (IOCG) deposits. Sub-types include Iron Skarn-type, Kiruna-
type, Olympic Dam-type, Cloncurry-type, Phalaborwa-type and Bayan Obo-type 
(Gandhi, 2004). Some deposits such as Olympic Dam in South Australia have isotopic 
evidence that suggest a genetic connection to mafic/ultramafic dykes and perhaps to 
large mafic/ultramafic bodies at depth.  
 

Placer accumulations of REE minerals occur in Tertiary or Quaternary strata 
where granitic or high-grade metamorphic rocks are broken down and their resistant 
heavy mineral assemblages are transported, sorted and deposited by river and/or 
coastal processes. Most commercial deposits are in marine sands and include zircon, 
monazite and xenotime (e.g., Eneabba, west coast Australia; Blind River, Ontario; 
Music Valley, southern California). 

 
 Large, low-grade deposits of REE occur in alkaline igneous rocks formed from 
alkali-rich magmas that precipitate sodium- and potassium-bearing minerals. Examples 
include REE-bearing loparite from nepheline syenite of the peralkaline Paleozic 
Lovozero massif, Russia, syenite-hosted intrusion at the HREE-zirconium deposit at 
Pajarito Mountain, New Mexico and the peralkaline granite complex at Strange Lake Zr-
HREE-Nb-Be deposit, Quebec.  
 
 Ion-adsorption REE deposits were recently recognized as residual deposits of 
REE-bearing clays. These deposits are associated with weathered REE-enriched 
granites in the Jiangxi Province of southern China. Rare earth elements released during 
granitic weathering (i.e, feldspar breakdown) are adsorbed by clays such as halloysite 
and kaolinite. A primary example includes the Longnan deposit in China where REE-
enriched clays range from 3 to 10 m thick, have generally low grade (0.03-0.35 wt. % 
total REO; Grauch and Mariano, 2008), are divided  into layers based on clay 
mineralogy, and are exploited economically because it is relatively easy to extract REE.  
 
8.3 POSSIBLE SCENARIOS FOR REE ACCUMULATION IN THE SECOND WHITE 
SPECKLED SHALE OF NORTHEASTERN ALBERTA 
 
 The absolute origin of REE in the SBH Property and Second White Speckled 
Shale is unknown. Spatial concurrences that should be considered in any paragenetic 
discussion of the Birch Mountains area of northeastern Alberta include the following.  
 

1. There is strong evidence that polymetallic enrichment of the Second White 
Speckled Shale in the SBH Property area is associated with hydrothermal-type 
mineralization (e.g., Dufresne et al. 2001; Dufresne et al., 2011). 

2. A depositional time gap, which is estimated to span 4 to 8 million years (Dufresne 
et al. 2001),  exists between the Second White Speckled Shale and the Late 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 38 
 

Cretaceous Lea Park Formation and may be temporally associated with 
extensional tectonics and kimberlite emplacement in the Birch Mountains (i.e., 
foreland bulge; Eccles 2011).   

3. The Birch Mountains area encompassing the SBH Property has been intruded by 
~78-72 Ma kimberlite to alkaline intrusive bodies that comprise evolved magmas 
enriched in light REE (LREE), carbonate and late-stage mineral assemblages for 
these types of rocks including apatite (Eccles, 2011).  

4. Beneath the sedimentary rocks of the Western Canadian Sedimentary Basin in 
the SBH Property area, the Taltson Magmatic Zone is defined by a 150–200 km 
wide zone that is dominated by 1.99–1.93 Ga granitoid rocks, including 
metaluminous to moderately peraluminous granitoids and moderate to strongly 
peraluminous granitoids (e.g.; Bostock et al., 1987, 1991). 
 

Consequently, broad-spectrum hypotheses of REE-enriched Second White Specks 
paragenesis could involve REE contribution from alkaline, A-type granite, hydrothermal, 
or any combination of these potential sources. Given we are dealing with REE-enriched 
shale another interesting supposition is ion-adsorption. These authors see no particular 
reason why any of the aforementioned potential sources couldn't create a similar REE 
ion-adsorption environment such as those occurring in southern China where 
weathered granitic rocks are the proposed original REE source. That is, under the right 
conditions of ion concentration, ionic strength, pH and cohesive energy density of the 
mineral sorbate, liberated REE could form adsorption bonds with the Second White 
Specks black organic-rich shale with smectitic horizons regardless of the ultimate REE 
source. 
 
9. EXPLORATION 
 

Exploration by DNI and APEX on behalf of DNI during 2010 and 2011 consisted 
of a surface sampling program to provide material for leaching test work and a diamond 
drilling program with drilling targeting the Buckton and Asphalt Mineralized Zones.  
Samples collected during the 2010 surface sampling program from outcrops of Second 
White Specks Shale at Asphalt-H and Gos-Cr-C have been archived and may be 
utilized in future for follow-up leaching test work. 
 

The results of the 2011 diamond drilling program are discussed in the following 
section. The 2011 drilling program in conjunction with data from historic drilling provided 
enough data for a mineral resource estimate calculation for the Buckton Mineralized 
Zone.  The resource estimate is provided and discussed in Section 14 below. 

 

10. DRILLING 2011 
 
10.1 – 2011 DRILLING SUMMARY 
 

1997 drill cores were reanalyzed and used in the REE-Y-Sc-Th resource 
estimate, but by far, the bulk of the data used in the resource model and calculation are 
from a 2011 DNI drill campaign conducted between January 19

th
 and February 14

th
, 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 39 
 

2011. Lone Peak Drilling ("Lone Peak") of Kimberly, British Columbia was contracted to 
provide drilling services, and APEX was retained by DNI to provide drilling supervision, 
core logging and processing services.  
 

A more thorough account of the 2011 drilling was documented by Dufresne et al. 
(2011) and is summarized here. A total of eight HQ diameter vertical diamond drillholes 
were completed during the period.  Five drillholes were collared at the Buckton 
Mineralized Zone (Figures 10.1). The remaining three holes were drilled south of 
Buckton at the Asphalt Mineralized Zone.  The eight core holes yielded a total of 
approximately 648 m of drill core. All five Buckton drillholes intersected the Second 
White Specks Shale with formation widths of between 11.1 and 23 m (Table 10.1). The 
drill core boxes were removed from the field by Lone Peak and are stored at an 
unheated storage facility in Edmonton. Drill core logging and sampling was conducted 
by APEX staff under the supervision of Mr. Dufresne and Mr. Eccles in Edmonton. See 
Dufresne et al. (2011) for a complete set of 2011 drill logs.

 The Second White Speckled Shale Formation in drillholes 11BK-01, 11BK-02 
and 11BK-03 has a consistent thickness of between 20 m to 23 m. In contrast the 
Second White Speckled Shale Formation in drillholes 11BK-04 and 11BK-05 has 
revised thicknesses of between 13 and 14 m. Upon further core investigation, in no way 
does it appear that the Second White Speckled Shale Formation in 11BK-04 and 11BK-
05 are reduced in thickness due to compaction. Rather, the upper portion of the Second 
White Speckled Shale Formation in these two drillholes has essentially been 
decapitated. This is supported by the missing whitish-weathering metal-rich sulphide 
zone and bentonite-rich shale typical of the uppermost portion of the Second White 
Speckled Shale Formation. In addition, the high angle of bedding in the overlying 
LaBiche Formation, co-mingling of LaBiche and Second White Speckled Shale 
Formation directly above the Second White Speckled Shale Formation, and movement 
features including slickenside surfaces in the co-mingling unit provide lithological and 
textural evidence that further supports detachment of the upper portion of the Second 
White Speckled Shale Formation in 11BK-04 and 11BK-05. To explain this detachment,  



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 40 
 

 
Figure 10.1: 2011 Buckton Drillhole Locations (11BK01 to 11BK05). 1997 drillholes include 7BK01 
to 7BK05.  

 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 41 
 

  
it is possible that bentonite zones acted as pliable surfaces that further influenced slump 
surfaces during syndepositional or postdepositional topographic modification of the 
area. It is also important to point out that the potential slumping is restricted in 
geographic area to 11BK-04 and 11BK-05 and so far not diagnostic of the Buckton 
Mineralized Zone.   
 
10.2 – 2011 CORE SAMPLING SUMMARY 

 
A total of 674 individual samples were collected from the 2011 drill core (Table 

10.2). Samples intervals of 0.5 m in the Second White Speckled Shale Formation and 1 
m or more in LaBiche and Belle Fourche formations were used to test the core. It was 
agreed before logging began that individual bentonite beds and bone beds should not 
be split between samples; thus, some samples are slightly longer or shorter than 
surrounding samples, so as to keep these individual beds within a single sample. Care 
was taken that samples never straddled formation boundaries. Generally, samples 
smaller than 25 cm or larger than 25 cm over the normal length (0.5 m or 1.0 m) were 
avoided. 

 
Additional sample preparation for Second White Specks Shale samples included 

the post-pulverizing removal of two 50 g subsamples for an archive split retained by 
DNI. The analytical methodology employed by DNI includes: 
 

1) Insertion of analytical blanks from a previously Actlabs-prepared batch of 
homogenized matrix-matched material. A total of 35 blanks were added at an 
analytical sample sequence of approximately one blank per every 50 samples for 
non-Second White Specks Shale samples and one blank per every 10 samples 
for samples of the Second White Specks Shale.  

2) For Second White Speckled Shale samples, two additional duplicate splits were 
created and analyzed separately: i) a duplicate subsample from the pulp; and ii) 
a second subsample from the coarse reject.  

 
A standard blank was created from a 10.7 kilogram sample of LaBiche Shale 

from the 1997 drill core in order to match the shale matrix for the 2011 drilling program 
and is referred to as the "LaBiche Blank". The material was originally shipped to 
ActLabs in 2009 from Edmonton. The material was crushed, pulverized and 
homogenized. A number of cuts were analyzed with ActLabs package 1H2 (Gold +53 by 
four acid ICP and INA) and for carbon and sulphur. The Labiche Shale Blank was then 
separated into multiple 50 g aliquots and used as a matrix matched blank in the 2011 
core assaying program.  

 
Samples were grouped into three general batches for analytical work: 1) 

Analytical Lot SB110427 (n=143 samples) includes all samples, regardless of formation 
or lithology, from the Asphalt area; 2) Analytical Lot SB110429 (n=261 samples) all 
Second White Speckled Shale Formation samples from the Buckton Mineralized Zone; 
and 3) Analytical Lot SB110426 (n=270 samples) includes all non-Second White 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 42

11. SAMPLE PREPARATION, ANALYSIS AND SECURITY 
 
11.1 RE-SAMPLING 1997 DRILL CORE  
 

Considerable sample material from 1990's Tintina exploration work, including an 
archival split of drill cores from Tintina's 1997 drilling program, is currently archived in 
storage at the Alberta Geological Survey (AGS) Mineral and Core Research Facility 
(MCRF) in Edmonton, Alberta. The samples collectively provide a broad variety of 
duplicate sample material, all of which are available for reference, verification and future 
test work.  
 

In July 2009, arrangements were made with the MCRF to allow DNI to collect 
samples from the archived drill core. During resampling, DNI noted no significant 
discrepancy between downhole lithologies between the original drill logs and the 
archived core boxes. In addition, there was no evidence of disruption or missing core 
which might be expected to compromise core sample integrity. The MCRF retains no 
formal record of past review of the core by others. A total of 20 samples were collected 
from Buckron drillholes 7BK01, 7BK03, 7BK04 and 7BK05 (Table 11.1). The majority of 
the samples are of Second White Specks Shale, however, a select handful of samples 
were taken from the Shaftesbury Formation (Belle Fourche Member) beneath the 
Second White Specks Shale and a single long intercept of LaBiche Shale (from 7BK04) 
overlying the Second White Specks Shale was collected for the purposed of creating a 
matrix blank for future analytical test work.

11.2 – SURFACE SAMPLING LABORATORY INSTRUCTIONS, ANALYSIS AND 
QUALITY CONTROL 
 

During 2009 and 2010, a surface sampling program conducted by personnel 
from APEX under the supervision of Mr. S. Sabag of DNI and Mr. M. Dufresne of APEX, 
was completed to provide bulk material for bioleach extraction test work. While these 
samples are not used in this REE-Y-Sc-Th resource estimate technical report, a brief 
background is provided as due diligence regarding the metallurgical leaching test work. 
 

The helicopter supported sampling program collected black shale material in the 
form of continuous channel (trench) samples from creek exposures. Samples were 
collected into thick plastic poly bags and secured with cable ties. All samples were 
collected in duplicate. The samples were transported to Fort McMurray via helicopter 
and stored at the secure Highland Helicopters hanger site. The samples were then 
transported to Edmonton by APEX personnel to the APEX warehouse. While in 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 44 
 

Edmonton, the samples were organized, catalogued, and prepared for shipment. The 
samples were either sent by APEX to Actlabs of Ancaster Ontario for geochemical 
analysis or were delivered by APEX personnel to the AITF laboratory in Edmonton. At 
all times the samples were in the possession of APEX staff or in our locked office and 
warehouse facility maintaining chain of custody on the samples until such time as the 
sample were delivered to a laboratory.   
 
11.3 – ORIGINAL 2011 DRILL CORE ANALYSIS 
 

A complete description of the 2011 drill core sampling, laboratory instructions, 
analysis and quality control is provided in Dufresne et al. (2011), and summarized as 
follows.  

 
Drill core samples (n=674) were sent for geochemical analysis to Actlabs of 

Ancaster, Ontario. The analysis consisted of bulk density (specific gravity [SG]), 
Instrumental Neutron Activation Analysis (INAA), inductively coupled plasma (ICP) 
analysis following an aqua regia sample digestion, ICP analysis following a total 
digestion in four acids sample preparation, analysis for carbon (C-org) and sulphur (S) 
species by combustion (Leco) and Infrared (IR) analyses. The analytical results of the 
re-sampling of historic core generally correlated well with historical results, with the 
exception of consistently lower organic carbon (C-org), higher bromine (Br) and higher 
SG. Complete analytical results and tables comparing the 2009 re-sample results to 
original 1997 historic drill core results are provided in an assessment report prepared by 
Sabag (2010). 
 

 
11.4 – RE-ANALYZING 1997 DRILL CORE AND 2011 DRILL CORE FOR RARE 
EARTHS 
 
 During 2011, a complete set of the 2011 drill core samples (n=674) plus the re-
sampled 1997 drill core (n=24) were re-analyzed for REE's. Thus a total of 698 samples 
were re-analyzed in Analytical Lot# SB110920. For sample material, Actlab's used pulp 
in hand, where available, or pulverized new pulp from reject on hand. A complete suite 
of REE, Y, Sc and Th were analyzed by Actlab's analytical package Code 8 – REE 
Assay Package, the results of which are presented in Appendix 1.  
 

Actlab's Code 8 – REE assay package uses Fusion ICP & ICP/MS. The analysis 
requires that the samples be ground to 95%-200 mesh to ensure complete fusion of 
resistate minerals. The analysis requires a lithium metaborate/tetraborate fusion with 
subsequent analysis by ICP and ICP/MS. In no circumstances was an acid digestion of 
any type used. Mass balance is required as an additional quality control technique and 
elemental totals of the oxides should be between 98 to 101%. Detection limits for 
elements analyzed by ICP/MS, in parts per million, include La (0.1), Ce (0.1), Pr (0.05), 
Nd (0.1), Sm (0.1), Eu (0.05), Gd (0.1), Tb (0.1), Dy (0.1), Ho (0.1), Er (0.1), Tm (0.05), 
Yb (0.1), Lu (0.04) and Th (0.1). Detection limits for elements analyzed by ICP, in parts 
per million, include Y (2) and Sc (1).  



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 45 
 

 
12. DATA VERIFICATION 
 
12.1 – 2009 DRILL CORE RESAMPLING 
 

The 2009 resampling has reproduced—with excellent correlation—the results of 
the original 1997 sampling program for 3 out of the 6 Second Whites Specks Shale 
intersections in the Buckton Resource area. Based upon these results the original 1997 
core assays are considered of excellent quality are useable for the purposes of the 
Buckton Mineralized Zone resource estimate. 
 

As part of DNI's verification program and their overall QA/QC program, 
arrangements were made with the AGS Mineral Core Research Facility (MCRF) to allow 
DNI to collect samples from the archived 1997 Tintina drill core during 2009. The 
resampling relied on the original drill logs and the driller's depth footage wooden block 
markers in the core boxes to determine sample intercepts (see Table 11.1). Actlabs 
inserted a series of blank samples, internal pulp duplicates, and preparation standards 
into the sample stream.  No issues were detected in results from the Actlabs QA/QC 
samples. 
 

An analysis of the results of the 2009 historic core resampling program versus 
the original assays obtained for the 1997 core sampling is presented in Dufresne et al. 
(2011; Figures 12.24 to 12.30). Linear regression analysis shows a correlation of better 
than 95 %.  
 
12.2 – 2010 SURFACE AND 2011 DRILL CORE SAMPLING 
 

The 2010 and 2011 sampling and assaying program produced sample 
information that meets industry standards for accuracy and reliability. The assay results 
are sufficiently accurate and precise for use in resource estimation and the release of 
drillhole results on a hole by hole basis. 
 

For a complete discussion on data verification, the reader is directed to Dufresne 
et al. (2011). In summary, data collected during the 2010 surface sampling and 2011 
diamond drilling programs were checked for veracity. The QA/QC procedures and 
results of the 2009 and 2010 drill programs were reviewed independently by Michael 
Dufresne, P.Geol., principal of APEX Geoscience Ltd.  
 

The standard reference material analyses indicated that the assay process is 
producing valid results. The Labiche Blank assays show that there has been no 
contamination in the preparation or assay process. The Labiche Blank was inserted a 
total of 34 times amongst the 674 drill core samples that were analyzed during 2011 
(one LaBiche Blank every 10 samples). The standard practice was to insert a Labiche 
Blank every 10 samples within the Second White Specks Shale sample stream and 
every 50 samples in the sample stream for overburden (till), Labiche and Belle Fourche 
shales. The data from the 34 Labiche Blank analyses were compared to the average 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 46 
 

value for all of the Labiche Blank analyses for the metals of interest (see Figures 12.1 to 
12.8 in Dufresne et al., 2011). To establish an acceptable range for the results, a 
plus/minus of two standard deviations from the average value were plotted along with 
the results from the 34 standard analyses from the 2011 drilling program. There is little 
variation in the individual analyses for most of the metals of interest versus the average 
value of the Labiche Blank and there are few failures that are observable.   
 

Assays for the pulp and reject duplicates confirm the values in the original 
assays. A pulp duplicate and a reject duplicate were inserted every 10 samples in 
Actlabs' sample stream. The results of all the pulp and reject duplicates for the metals of 
interest are plotted in a series of scatter plots (see Figures 12.9 to 12.16 in Dufresne et 
al., 2011). The scatter plots show the accuracy and precision of the pulp and reject 
duplicates for all the metals of interest is excellent. The two sets of assays produce 
nearly the same values for all the metals. 
 

Actlabs inserted a series of SRM sample standards (GXR-1, GXR-4, GXR-6, 
SDC-1, SCO-1, DNC-1 and OREAS-13b) into each stream of Second White Specks 
Shale, overburden (till), Labiche and Belle Fourche samples. Results for the standards 
for most of the metals fall within the control limits at the prescribed 90% confidence rate. 
Scatter plots for all the standards' certified values versus their measured values show 
that most of the standards provided reasonable results for most of the metals (see 
Figures 12.17 to 12.23 in Dufresne et al., 2011).   
 

The drillhole database was also validated. The drillhole data were entered into 
MICROMINE

TM
 by APEX personnel. Using the software's drillhole database validation 

function, the data was checked for overlapping sample intervals, downhole survey data 
that deviated beyond acceptable limits as well as collar and hole length data.  
 
 
13. MINERAL PROCESSING AND METALURGICAL TESTING 
 

In 2009 DNI commenced an extensive sampling and metallurgical test work 
program focused on acid and bioleaching studies in order to assess the recoverability of 
metals from the Second White Specks Shale using various leaching techniques.  The 
test work was completed using vertical trench samples that were collected from Second 
White Specks Shale outcrops in 2009-2010 by APEX. These samples and composites 
of the samples were then used in a variety of leaching test programs at several different 
facilities using a number of techniques.  The leaching test work is extensive and much 
of the bioleaching work is complicated and involves various partial extractions. The 
procedures and results are well summarized by Sabag (2010) and in DNI corporate 
news releases between 2010 and 2011 (DNI News Releases dated March 4, 2010; April 
28, 2010 and September 8, 2011). The reader is also referred to a summary of the 
pertinent leaching test work conducted by DNI to date in Dufresne et al. (2011). Only 
the extraction estimates are provided in this report.   
 
 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 47 
 

13.1 - 2009 – 2010 SULPHURIC ACID LEACHING TEST WORK 
 

Sulphuric acid leaching tests were conducted at Actlabs from late 2009 to 
February 2010 on three representative samples of Second White Speckled Formation 
black shale (Samples 09ASH004S, 09ASH005S and 09ASH006S). The sulphuric acid 
leaching tests completed by DNI reported recoveries from 36-hour leaching at 30

o
C as 

follows (results from 24-hour leaching at 50
o
C also shown in brackets): Mo-28% (51%); 

Ni-89% (70%); U-84% (67%); V-34% (51%); Zn-88% (70%); Cu-53% (57%); Co-86% 
(69%); Cd-93% (71%); Li-45% (58%; DNI News Release March 4, 2010). 
 

DNI concluded that the test results were significant as they served to 
demonstrate: (1) that metals could be readily extracted from the black shales; (2) that 
recoveries, even though not optimized, were high enough to compel expansion of the 
test work and to continue its advance toward scaled up bench tests and column 
leaching; and, (3) that metal recoveries can be enhanced by optimizing test conditions 
(DNI News Release March 4, 2010). 
 
13.2 - 2009 BIOLEACHING TEST WORK - BRGM 
 

During 2009, bioleaching tests conducted by the Bureau de Recherches 
Géologiques et Minières (BRGM) in France. Bioleaching on sample 09ASH004 (1,330g) 
showed that the Second White Specks black shale had good metal extraction 
recoveries (Table 13.1; DNI News Release April 28, 2010). The results were reported to 
demonstrate that metals can be extracted from the black shales with the bioleaching 
technique.  
 

The BRGM also completed a duplicate parallel test without the addition of bio-
organisms (abiotic), to evaluate the effects of biotic intermediation on metals recoveries. 
The leaching results concluded that while biological activity may not be as important for 
metal solubilization as previously thought, it is most likely responsible for improvement 
in metal yields during leaching. In conclusion, the tests demonstrated that metal 
recoveries of the black shales are high.

13.3 - 2009–2010 BIOLEACHING TEST WORK – ALBERTA INNOVATES 
TECHNOLOGY FUTURES 
 

Bioleaching tests were carried out by Alberta Innovates Technology Futures 
(AITF) formerly the Alberta Research Council to the specifications of DNI and Dr. Corale 
Brierly (C.L. Brierly Consultancy LLC, Denver, USA). The tests were completed in 
stages from late 2009 to 2010.  Unlike the bioleaching tests carried out by the BRGM, 
which utilized a bacterial culture from BRGM's culture bank, the tests at the AITF 
utilized bacterial culture harvested from the Second White Specks Shale, which had 
been adapted to the shale over several weeks. 
 

The results of the AITF studies sample BK456 show that suitable bioleaching 
organisms are indigenous to the shale and can be readily extracted, cultivated, and 
adapted to the shale's chemistry (DNI News Release September 8, 2011). Additionally, 
these studies confirmed that a broad suite of metals can be collectively extracted from 
the shale. Metals such as Ni, U, Zn, Co and Cd were demonstrated to have high 
recoveries, while Mo, V, Cu, and Li had lower recoveries (Table 13.2; Sabag, 2010). It is 
believed that optimal leaching parameters could improve these recoveries. The results 
from these studies were consistent with results from the bioleaching tests completed by 
the BRGM in France.

13.4 - 2009 - 2010 - MLA MINERAL STUDY 
 

Following the geochemical analysis, in October of 2009, a total of 15 samples of 
the Second Whites Specks black shale (09ASH001S to 09ASH006S, 09GS1-001S to 
09GS1-003S, 09GS2-001S and 09GS3-001S to 09GS3-001S) were sent to Actlabs 



Technical Report, Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast AlbertaPage 49 
 

Geometallurgy Services (a division of Actlabs) in Ancaster, ON, for a full mineralogical 
characterization via a Mineral Liberation Analyser (MLA) Study (Sabag, 2010; Hamilton, 
2010). 
 

A MLA600F scanning electron microprobe (SEM) was utilized in order to obtain a 
detailed account of metallic mineral characteristics in terms of type and mineralogical 
variance. The MLA Study was considered to have fallen short of expectations, which 
were to provide the subsequent leaching test work with definitive quantitative 
information as to mineral speciation and their abundances. The work did, however, 
confirm the black shale hosted metallic mineralization is typically too fine to afford 
identification by optical or EDS methods and that the metal-bearing compounds are 
dispersed throughout the shale as extremely tiny particles (often submicron) trapped in 
organic matter (or slimes). The study also suggested that many of the metals might 
occur as charged particles within oxides, hydroxides and clays, rather than as discrete 
mineral phases. This, latter, suggestion is supported by the ease with which metals can 
be leached from the shale as observed during the subsequent leaching and bioleaching 
test work and also by historic mineralogical work from the Property (noting instability of 
at least some of the mineralogy in the shale and its susceptibility to decomposition). 

 
Complete descriptions of laboratory procedures, analysis and results for the MLA 

Study are located in Hamilton (2010), which can be found in Sabag (2010). 
 
 
13.5 - 2010 RARE METALS LEACHING TEST WORK – ALBERTA INNOVATES 
TECHNOLOGY FUTURES 
 

In addition to collective recoverability of traditional metals from the shale, 
bioleaching studies carried out by AITF over the past two years have confirmed 
recoverability of REE including critical elements Dy, Y, Tb, Eu and Nd, and associated 
specialty metals such as Sc and Th (DNI Metals Inc. News Releases, September 2011 
and December 2011). Calculated recoveries of specialty metals and REE's based on 
results from the AITF bioleaching testwork studies are summarized below for sample 
BK456.

14. MINERAL RESOURCE ESTIMATE 
 
14.1 - INTRODUCTION 
 

The REE-Y-Sc-Th resource modelling for the Buckton Mineralized Zone was 
completed by Mr. R. Eccles, P.Geol, a Qualified Person with respect to mineral 
resource estimation as defined by NI 43-101.  The resource estimation and statistics 
were completed by Mr. S. Nicholls, MAIG under the direct supervision of Mr. M. 
Dufresne, P. Geol., who are both Qualified Persons with respect to mineral resource 
estimation as defined by National Instrument 43-101. Mineral resource modelling and 
estimation was carried out using a 3-dimensional block model based on geostatistical 
applications using commercial mine planning software MICROMINE (v12.5.2). 
 

The project limits area is based in the UTM coordinate system (NAD 1927; 
Zone12) using a parent block size of 250 m x 250 m x 2 m with sub-blocking down to 25 
m x 25 m x 1 m. The Buckton Mineralized Zone REE-Y-Sc-Th resource modeling 
utilized 6 historic core holes completed in 1997 and 5 core holes completed in 2011.  
Mr. M. Dufresne, P.Geol, supervised both the 1997 and 2011 drilling campaigns along 
with logging and sampling of the drill core during both campaigns. Grade (assay) and 
geologic information is derived from work conducted by APEX personnel during the 
1997 and 2011 field seasons. The Buckton Mineralized Zone is contained entirely within 
the Second White Speckled (2WS) Shale; an extensive basin wide black shale that is a 
known marker horizon within the Western Canadian Sedimentary Basin. 
 

The REE-Y-Sc-Th mineral resource for the Buckton Mineralized Zone is reported 
in accordance with the Canadian Securities Administrators National Instrument 43-101 
and has been estimated using the CIM "Estimation of Mineral Resources and Mineral 
Reserves Best Practice Guidelines" dated November 23

rd
, 2003 and CIM "Definition 

Standards for Mineral Resources and Mineral Reserves" dated November 27
th

, 2010.  
Mineral resources are not mineral reserves and do not have demonstrated economic 
viability.  There is no guarantee that all or any part of the mineral resource will be 
converted into a mineral reserve.   
 
14.2 - DATA 
 

14.2.1 - Data Summary and Histograms 
 

There are 17 elements of interest that were defined for the Buckton Mineralized 
Zone REE-Y-Sc-Th resource. These include La, Ce, Pr, Nd, Pr, Sm, Eu, Gd, Tb, Dy, 
Ho, Er, Tm, Yb and Lu.  In addition, Yttrium (Y), Scandium (Sc) and Thorium (Th) were 
also reviewed. Histograms and summary statistics were calculated for the Buckton 
Mineralized Zone (Table 14.1 and Figure 14.1). Most of the elements display bi-modal 
populations. Upon domaining out the high and low grade populations, it was observed 
that some of these populations still exhibit characteristics of multiple populations.  Due 
to the limited number of drill holes and resulting samples present, it was deemed 
inappropriate to break the data up further.

14.2.2 - Database Validation 
 

The 1997 drill holes were positioned using topographic maps and topography.  
The holes were subsequently surveyed in 1997 and subsequently during 2011 using a 
hand held Garmin GPS unit in UTM coordinates, NAD 1927, Zone 12 Datum. The new 
2011 collars were surveyed in using a hand held Garmin GPS unit in UTM coordinates, 
NAD 1927, Zone 12 Datum.  The elevations of the drill holes were initially obtained by 
using the hand held Garmin GPS, however, they have been subsequently modified for 
all 11 drill holes by using available high resolution Light Detection and Ranging (LiDar) 
technology with 1 m resolution. 
 

Collar sighting pickets for the 1997 and 2011 drill holes were clearly marked with 
the drillhole number, the dip and termination depth, using a permanent felt marker. All 
11 drill holes were vertical holes; no down hole surveying was employed. Upon 
completion of each hole, the casing was removed and the drill site reclaimed.   
 

All drill logs, summaries, survey data and analytical results from the 1997 and 
2011 programs are kept in a master DNI drilling database. Drill core logging was 
completed in Microsoft excel format, with hardcopy, pdf and digital back-ups. Drill data, 
cross sections and 3D plots are interpreted and generated in Edmonton using, excel 
and MICROMINE software.  The 1997 and 2011 drill core were logged and sampled by 
APEX personnel under the direct supervision of either Mr. M. Dufresne or Mr. R. Eccles.   
 

At the end of the 2011 program, the excel drillhole database was copied into 
MICROMINE by APEX personnel. Using Micromine's drillhole database validation 
function, the data was checked for overlapping sample and geological intervals, as well 
as survey, collar and hole length data.  A few minor discrepancies were found and fixed 
within the database promptly.    
 

All 11 drill holes were manually checked and validated for collar, survey, geology 
and assay data.  Collar data was compared back to values on the original drill logs.  
Lithology codes were compared to original drill logs and assay results were compared 
to laboratory certificates. The database is considered reliable for mineral resource 
estimation purposes. 
 
14.2.3 - MICROMINE Database 
 

The drilling database used is current up to September 1
st
, 2011.  The database 

incorporates all available diamond drilling and analytical data.  All data for the mineral 
resource estimation was copied from excel into Micromine format.  The four main 
MICROMINE .dat files that were utilized were: 

 Buckton collars – Collar file 

 Buckton REE assays – Survey file 

 Buckton geology – Geology file 

 LiDar 10m BE xyz – Surface topography 



Technical Report, Buckton REE Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast Alberta, Page 62 
 

 

 
There were a total of 11 diamond drillholes within the export of which all were used 

to guide the geological interpretation but only 8 were used in the REE-Y-Sc-Th resource 
estimation.  Spacing between drillholes varies from 240 m to 2.5 km, with an average of 
about 1 km between drillholes.  There were four drill lines that ranged in spacing from 
670 m to 2 km.   
 

The Buckton REE-Y-Sc-Th assays file comprises 549 analyses of variable length for 
all the sampled lithologies, but when composited in MICROMINE yielded a database of 
302 sample composites for the Second White Specks Shale that were used for the 
REE-Y-Sc-Th mineral resource estimation. Table 14.1 provides a statistical summary of 
REE, Y, Sc and Th from continuous sampling of DNI's 2011 drill core and re-analysis of 
their 1997 drill core on a selected set of 1997 samples. The 549 analyses are separated 
by lithology into 200 samples from the Second White Speckled Shale, 207 samples of 
the overlying Labiche Formation and 142 samples of the underlying Belle Fourche 
Formation. The Second White Speckled Shale is further separated into two groups: a 

high REE-enriched group of 72 samples (average La-Lu, Y of 395 ppm), and a less 

REE-enriched group of 128 samples (average La-Lu, Y of 186 ppm). A subset of these 
data were selected for the resource estimate block modeling, however the premise of 
the block model, places emphasis on these two groups of varying REE content within 
the Second White Speckled Shale. 
 

Data supplied and utilized in MICROMINE included collar easting, northing and 
elevation coordinates, lithology information, and REE, Y, Sc and Th assay data, and 
bulk density data.  The collar co-ordinates were obtained by hand held GPS and the RL 
were assigned using the detailed 1 m spaced LiDar data.  All drill holes were drilled 
vertically and as such there are no down hole surveys.  Dip of the hole was set up using 
a clinometer after the drill was properly leveled. 
 

The drillhole database was validated and as such all sample duplicates and repeat 
duplicates were removed from the estimation sample file.  Other than the duplicate 
samples there were no errors identified. 
 
14.2.4 - Data Type Comparison 
 

As there has only been diamond drilling conducted at the Buckton Mineralized 
Zone a data type comparison is not required. Diamond drilling is considered a good 
quality drilling method and suitable for resources estimation.  
 
 
14.3 – QUALITY CONTROL 
 

The drillhole data collected during the 1997 and 2011 drilling programs were 
checked for veracity, then were entered into MICROMINE and were validated using the 
MICROMINE's drillhole validation tools.  The upper and lower boundaries of the 



Technical Report, Buckton REE Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast Alberta, Page 63 
 

 

Buckton Mineralized Zone within the 2WS Shale for the most part are easily identified in 
core and in the down hole trace element geochemistry as illustrated in Figure 14.2.  
 

A series of blanks, duplicates and standards were inserted in the sample 
shipments at Actlabs for the 2011 core samples. The results of the lab standards, 
blanks and duplicates were checked to ensure results were within acceptable limits.  No 
issues were identified. For the 1997 drillholes, three of the six drillholes were resampled 
and analyzed in 2009 including the entire Second White Specks Shale intersections. 
The 2009 resampling and analysis program confirmed the validity of the original 1997 
assays. The 2009 reanalyzed data from the 1997 drill cores were used in the REE-Y-
Sc-Th resource estimate. 
 
14.4 – LITHOLOGICAL MODEL/LODE INTERPRETATION 
 

The drillhole lithology was plotted and displayed next to the drill hole.  The 
geology displayed (from the top of the drillhole to the base) overburden, LaBiche 
Formation, Second White Specks Shale (SWS) and the Belle Fourche (Upper 
Shaftesbury) Formation.  The principle high REE mineralization is constrained to the 
lower half of the SWS. Therefore, two separate domains were used to constrain the 
Buckton Mineralized Zone and wireframes used for resource estimation purposes, a 
high REE domain and a low REE domain (Figure 14.2). 
 

The SWS mineralized wireframes were extended outwards 500 m away from the 
nearest drillhole to define the extremities of the wireframes and subsequent block 
model. Trimming the model to 500 m in the north east, east and south was not 
necessary as the SWS outcrops at surface along the eastern edge of the Birch 
Mountains.  The LiDar topography was used to trim the 2WS wireframes in these areas. 

14.5 – ASSAY SUMMARY STATISTICS 

 

The 17 REE, Y, Sc and Th elements were converted to oxide equivalents as per 
Table 14.2. It was evident from the histograms that 15 out of the total 17 oxides 
exhibited mixed populations of assay data (Figure 14.1). As a result of the multiple 
populations it was decided to wireframe out the two populations to produce a high grade 
and a low grade domain for each of the fifteen oxides. 
 

Correlations between the various metal grades were calculated for the SWS 
shale mineralization.  There is a very strong correlation between all oxides except for 
Sc2O3 and ThO2 of which display a moderate correlation to each other (Table14.3).  It 
should be noted that there were three holes out of the total of 11 drill holes that have not 
been assayed for REE analysis.

14.6 – DRILLHOLE FLAGGING AND COMPOSITING 
  

Drillhole samples that were situated within the 2WS mineralized wireframe were 
selected and flagged with the wireframe name/code.   
 

The flagged samples were checked visually next to the drillhole to check that the 
automatic flagging process worked correctly. All samples were correctly flagged and 
there was no need to manually flag or remove any samples.  
 

A review of the sample lengths was conducted, looking at the sample width of the 
diamond drilling data.  The results showed a variable sample length from 0.23 m to 6.2 
m in length (Table 14.4).  The 2011 drillhole samples were collected for the most part at 
a standard sample length of 0.5 m. The 1997 drillhole sample lengths provide most of 
the variability in sample lengths. Looking at the samples, the dominant sample length 
was 0.5 m which makes up 83.1% of the data.  There was an addition 5 samples that 
were less than 0.5 m in length.  Essentially 85.7% of the sample data is less than 0.5 m 
in length. It was decided that 0.5 m should be used for a composited sample length. 
 

Length weighted composites were calculated for all of the oxides of interest in the 
2WS wireframe. The compositing process starts from the first point of intersection 
between the drillhole and the 2WS mineralized horizon, and is halted upon the end of 
the mineralized wireframe.   
 
Table 14.4: Sample Length Statistics for the SWS Shale Domain 

  Uncomposited Width 

Number 200 

Minimum 0.23 

Maximum 6.2 

    

Mean 0.782 

Median 0.5 

Std Dev 0.999 

 
Upon completion of the 0.5 m compositing it was decided to examine the 

remaining samples less than 0.5 m in length to determine if they would unduly bias the 
estimation.  This included only 10 composites less than 0.5 m and the decision to leave 
them in the composite file was made as it did not bias the oxide grades either way.  
Once complete this produced 302 samples (of the 549 total REE analyses) to be used 
in the estimation process.  
  

The compositing process did apply a slight positive bias to the average grade of 
the oxides, of which was due to the large sample sizes in the 1997 drill core resampling.  
Although a slight positive bias was observed in the compositing process it was decided 
to continue with 0.5 m composites as the removal of the larger sample size composites 
would eliminate three out of the total eight drill holes used in the REE-Y-Sc-Th 



Technical Report, Buckton REE Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast Alberta, Page 67 
 

 

estimation (Table 14.5).  It is recommended that if remaining diamond core is available 
that these large composite samples be reanalyzed at smaller sample lengths to 
eliminate any further bias in future REE-Y-Sc-Th estimations. The composited samples 
were used for all sample statistics, capping, estimation input file and validation 
comparisons.

combination of histograms, probability plots and inflection points were used to 
determine the extreme values to be cut.  During the estimation the extreme values were 
capped to the values provided in Table 14.6.

14.8 – GRADE CONTINUITY 
 

The variography utilized the composite data within the mineralized SWS black 
shale domain to produce spherical semi variogram's. There were insufficient samples to 
conduct variography on each individual oxide population and as such samples from 
both populations for each oxide element were used to determine continuity and 
orientation of mineralisation.  Some difficulties were encountered with the semi 
variogram's for some of the elements due to the limited number of drill holes, large 
spacing and irregular frequency of drilling.  Table 14.7 provides the search classification 
and the limits used in the estimation process.  The individual semi variograms are 
provided in Appendix 2. 
 

The down hole geochemical pattern for most elements contained within the SWS 
is similar and fairly predictable from drillhole to drillhole.  The pattern is reproduced from 
hole to hole and speaks to tremendous lateral continuity geochemically. For many 
oxides there is horizontal continuity that stretches hundreds of meters to kilometres with 
little change. However, the geochemistry can change significantly over a few meters 
vertically.  This suggests there is an inherent stratigraphic control to the mineralization. 
 



Technical Report, Buckton REE Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast Alberta, Page 69

In terms of grade continuity, the lateral continuity of mineralization versus the 

vertical continuity indicated is confirmed by the variography, however, there is 
substantial variation in the lateral continuity that may be real or may be due to the wide-
spaced nature of the drilling and a lack of information.  Table 14.7 shows the ranges in 
lateral continuity identified from the variography.  Most oxides exhibit grade continuity 
around 2 km, whereas Sc2O3 and ThO2 show horizontal grade continuity of 1 km and 
4.8 km respectively.  It is unclear if or how this will change with additional drilling. 
 

14.9 – SEARCH ELLIPSOIDS 
 

The search directions and size of the ellipsoids used in the estimation process 
were largely based on the Variography (Table 14.8). Each grade element was looked at 
individually and the search ellipsoid was tailored to the orientations identified in the 
variography. These search orientations honour the geological interpretation of the 
horizontal nature of the SWS stratigraphy and observed geochemical domains (Table 
14.8).

Buckton area. The samples were tagged with the formation name and then were 
separated into the different formations for use in the estimation process (Table 14.9).

14.11 – BLOCK MODEL EXTENTS AND BLOCK SIZE 
 

As a result of the wide drillhole spacing's a model block size of 250 m x 250 m x 
2 m was chosen for the Buckton Mineralized Zone REE-Y-Sc-Th resource estimate. 
The block model extents were extended far enough past the mineralized wireframe to 
encompass the entire mineralized shale domain.   
 

Table 14.10 presents the coordinate ranges and block size dimensions used to 
build the 3D block models from the mineralization wireframe. Sub-blocking was used to 
more effectively honour the volumes and shapes created during the geological 
interpretation of the mineralized wireframe or lode. There were a total number of 10,483 
blocks in the model. Each block was coded with the different populations as to enable 
estimation of the two populations separately. 
 
Table 14.10: Block Model Extents and Cell Dimensions for the SWS Shale Domain 

 

Deposit Block Model Dimensions Easting Northing RL 

Buckton Maximum 451550 6403900 682 

 Minimum 445500 6396650 570 

 Parent Cell Size 250 250 2 

 Sub Blocking Cell Size 25 25 1 

 
Upon setup of the block model the volumes of each lode was cross checked with 

the volume of the wireframe to check there was no significant discrepancies between 
the two. The block volume was only 0.03% different from the wireframe volume. 
 
 

14.12 – GRADE ESTIMATION 
 

The Buckton Mineral Zone grade estimation was calculated using ordinary 
kriging (OK) for each REE-Y-Sc-Th oxide. No trends were applied to the OK grade 
estimation. The kriging parameters were based on the variography conducted on the 
individual grade elements within the SWS shale domain. Estimation was only calculated 
on parent blocks. All sub blocks within the parent block were assigned the parent block 
grade. A block discretization of 5 x 5 x 2 was applied to all blocks during kriging. The 
two different grade populations for each oxide element were treated as a hard 
boundaryies, which meant that only samples within that grade population were used to 
estimate the grade of the each oxide in the respective blocks.  
 

There were four passes of estimation conducted for each population. For 
example four passes for the high grade population of La2O3 and four for the low grade 
population. The size of the elliptical search ellipsoid was based on the suggested 
ranges obtained from variography. The estimation criteria for each pass are provided in 
Table 14.11.

14.13 – EXPECTED RECOVERY AND METAL PRICES 
 

The SWS mineralized black shale is low grade "poly-metallic" black shale. It is 
likely that not one of the metals alone at current metal prices could provide a stand-
alone case for the economics of the Buckton Mineralized Zone. It will likely require 
several metals to drive the economics of the deposit in future, therefore, converting all 
of the metals into a single metal equivalent to look at the overall grade and value of the 
deposit does not provide a reasonable picture for the resource estimate. In addition, the 
amount of overburden, the expected amount of recoverable metal and the price for each 
metal (or oxide) will play a critical role in the final resource estimate.  As a result, each 
block and sub-block in the block model was assigned a cumulative $USD value based 
upon the estimated metal grade for each metal for that block, the expected potential 
recovery for each metal along with the $USD one to three year average price for that 
metal multiplied by the recoverable grade for each metal.  
 

Listed below in Table 14.12 are the assumed metal recoveries utilized for the 
Buckton Mineralized Zone REE-Y-Sc-Th resource estimate. The metal recoveries are 
based upon metallurgical test work conducted by DNI, which is summarized in the 
previous section on Mineral Processing and Metallurgical Testing and in a number of 
DNI news releases (DNI News Releases Dated March 4, 2010; April 28, 2010 and 
September 8, 2011). These recoveries have been rounded off with conservative 
estimates used from DNI's metallurgical test work then applied to the estimated metal 
grade for each block in the OK block model.   
 
Table 14.12: Metal Recoveries Used for the Buckton Mineralized Zone Resource Estimate 

Ce Dy Er Eu Gd La Nd Pr Sm Tb Y Yb Th Sc

Per solution (%) 77 92 88 83 88 66 66 58 91 97 90 90 100 51

Per solids (%) 68 92 92 86 90 70 78 76 83 92 92 64 74 57

Value used (%) 70 90 90 85 85 70 70 65 85 90 90 75 80 55  
 

The marketing of REE is commonly priced in the oxide form and analytical data 
are usually expressed in weight percentage of a particular element (Sinton 2005). Rare 
earth elements are not exchange-traded in the same way that precious (for instance, 
gold and silver) or non-ferrous metals (such as nickel, tin, copper, and aluminum) are. 



Technical Report, Buckton REE Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast Alberta, Page 73 
 

 

Instead they are sold on the private market, which makes their prices difficult to monitor 
and track. However, prices are published periodically on websites such as metal-
prices.com and asianmetal.com. The 17 elements are not usually sold in their pure 
form, but instead are distributed in mixtures of varying purity, e.g. "Neodymium metal ≥ 
99.5%". As such, pricing can vary based on the quantity and quality required by the end 
user's application. 
 

One to three year $USD average metal (or oxide) price were chosen for the 
resource estimate (Table 14.13). Three year average prices (November 20, 2008 to 
November 17, 2011) were available only for the LREE, MREE and Th. Alternatively, a 
one-year average prices (November 12, 2010 to November 20, 2011) were used for the 
HREE and Sc. Metal prices varied between sources, and in all conflicting instances, the 
lower pricing was used in the REE-Y-Sc-Th resource calculation.

14.14 – MODEL VALIDATION 
 
14.14.1 - Visual Validation 
 

The blocks were visually validated on cross sections comparing block grades 
versus the sample grades for all sections and drillholes (Figure 14.4).  In addition, the 
block and sample data were compared by grade element, easting and northing. These 
comparisons are presented in Table 14.14, Figure 14.5 and in Appendix 3.   
 

 
Figure 14.4: 11BK01 Cross-section Showing Ce2O3  Block Grades (ppm) versus Ce2O3 Sample 

Grades 



Technical Report, Buckton REE Resource Estimate, SBH Property, Northeast Alberta, Page 75 
 

 

 
14.14.2 - Statistical Validation 
 

Table 14.14 and Figure 14.5 show the average grade of the composited capped 
sample data versus the block model data. It can be concluded that the average and 
mean grade of the OK block model data is very close to or generally slightly lower than 
the sample data. This is the expected result for well behaved data and if the block 
model estimation process is being done correctly. The model data tends to have a 
reduced dispersion of the block grades resulting from the grade estimation process. The 
OK block modeling and estimation process tends to lower the high end grades 
compared to the sample data and increase the low end grades compared to the sample 
data. This is expected with the overall smoothing of the estimation process.
